2:29:1796:0:14:72:252:700:12:Times:12:Times:C:Firm Volatility in Granular Networks
2:30:1832:14:14:72:347:686:12:Times:12:Times:C:Bryan Kelly, Hanno Lustig, and Stijn Van Nieuwerburgh
2:31:1885:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:C:NBER Working Paper No. 19466
2:32:1913:14:14:72:150:658:12:Times:12:Times:C:September 2013
2:33:1927:14:28:72:152:644:12:Times:12:Times:C:JEL No. E1,G10
2:34:1941:28:28:306:339:616:12:Times:12:Times:C:ABSTRACT
2:35:1949:28:14:72:540:588:12:Times:12:Times:C:We propose a network model of firm volatility in which the customers’ growth rate shocks influence
2:36:2047:14:14:72:540:574:12:Times:12:Times:C:the growth rates of their suppliers, larger suppliers have more customers, and the strength of a customer-supplier
2:37:2161:14:14:72:540:560:12:Times:12:Times:C:link depends on the size of the customer firm. Even though all shocks are i.i.d., the network model
2:38:2260:14:14:72:540:546:12:Times:12:Times:C:produces firm-level volatility and size distribution dynamics that are consistent with the data. In the
2:39:2363:14:14:72:540:532:12:Times:12:Times:C:cross section, larger firms and firms with less concentrated customer networks display lower volatility.
2:40:2467:14:14:72:540:518:12:Times:12:Times:C:Over time, the volatilities of all firms co-move strongly, and their common factor is concentration
2:41:2566:14:14:72:540:504:12:Times:12:Times:C:of the economy-wide firm size distribution. Network effects are essential to explaining the joint evolution
2:42:2673:14:42:72:350:490:12:Times:12:Times:C:of the empirical firm size and firm volatility distributions.
2:43:2734:42:14:72:131:448:12:Times:12:Times:C:Bryan Kelly
2:44:2745:14:14:72:179:434:12:Times:12:Times:C:University of Chicago
2:45:2766:14:14:72:196:420:12:Times:12:Times:C:Booth School of Business
2:46:2790:14:14:72:205:406:12:Times:12:Times:C:5807 S. Woodlawn Avenue
2:47:2813:14:14:72:162:392:12:Times:12:Times:C:Chicago, IL 60637
2:48:2830:14:14:72:124:378:12:Times:12:Times:C:and NBER
2:49:2838:14:28:72:222:364:12:Times:12:Times:C:bryan.kelly@chicagobooth.edu
2:50:2866:28:14:72:138:336:12:Times:12:Times:C:Hanno Lustig
2:51:2878:14:14:72:269:322:12:Times:12:Times:C:UCLA Anderson School of Management
2:52:2912:14:14:72:233:308:12:Times:12:Times:C:110 Westwood Plaza, Suite C413
2:53:2942:14:14:72:216:294:12:Times:12:Times:C:Los Angeles, CA 90095-1481
2:54:2968:14:14:72:124:280:12:Times:12:Times:C:and NBER
2:55:2976:14:0:72:202:266:12:Times:12:Times:C:hlustig@anderson.ucla.edu
2:56:3001:0:14:306:424:448:12:Times:12:Times:C:Stijn Van Nieuwerburgh
2:57:3023:14:14:306:426:434:12:Times:12:Times:C:Stern School of Business
2:58:3047:14:14:306:410:420:12:Times:12:Times:C:New York University
2:59:3066:14:14:306:443:406:12:Times:12:Times:C:44 W 4th Street, Suite 9-120
2:60:3094:14:14:306:413:392:12:Times:12:Times:C:New York, NY 10012
2:61:3112:14:14:306:358:378:12:Times:12:Times:C:and NBER
2:62:3120:14:0:306:427:364:12:Times:12:Times:C:svnieuwe@stern.nyu.edu
3:63:3142:0:34:72:208:708:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:1    Introduction
3:64:3159:34:22:72:540:674:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Recent research has explored how the network structure and firm size distribution in an econ-
3:65:3252:22:21:72:540:652:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:omy can influence aggregate volatility. Acemoglu et al. (2012) and Carvalho (2010) show that
3:66:3344:21:22:72:540:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sparsity of inter-sector linkages inhibits diversification in an economy and raises aggregate
3:67:3437:22:22:72:540:609:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility.  Gabaix (2011) points out that “granularity,” or extreme skewness in firm sizes,
3:68:3529:22:21:72:540:587:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:concentrates economic mass among a few very large firms, similarly stifling diversification
3:69:3620:21:22:72:250:566:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and increasing aggregate volatility.
3:70:3656:22:22:89:539:544:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:This research is silent about the impact of networks and size concentration on the volatil-
3:71:3747:22:21:72:540:522:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ity of the firm.  The volatility of firm-level stock returns and cash flows varies greatly over
3:72:3842:21:22:72:540:501:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:time (Lee and Engle (1993)) and across firms (Black (1976), Christie (1982), and Davis et
3:73:3931:22:22:72:540:479:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:al.  (2007)).   Such  fluctuations  in  uncertainty  have  important  implications  for  investment
3:74:4030:22:21:72:540:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and hiring decisions and firm value, as highlighted by Bloom (2009).1But the underlying
3:75:4117:21:22:72:540:436:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:determinants  of  firm  volatility  are  poorly  understood.   In  much  of  the  work  on  volatility
3:76:4219:22:22:72:540:414:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in economics and finance,  firms are modeled to have heteroscedastic shocks without spec-
3:77:4308:22:21:72:540:392:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ifying  the  source  of  heteroscedasticity.   Our  goal  is  to  understand,  both  theoretically  and
3:78:4411:21:22:72:540:371:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:empirically, how inter-firm linkages and size distributions interact to endogenously produce
3:79:4503:22:22:72:252:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:heteroscedasticity at the firm level.
3:80:4540:22:21:89:539:327:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We propose a simple model in which firms are connected to other firms in a customer-
3:81:4624:21:22:72:540:306:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:supplier  network.   Firms’  idiosyncratic  growth  rate  shocks,  which  are  homoscedastic,  are
3:82:4722:22:22:72:540:284:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:transmitted in part to their trading partners.  Differences in firms’ network connections, and
3:83:4816:22:21:72:540:262:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:evolution of the network over time, impart total firm volatility with cross section and time
3:84:4908:21:22:72:196:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:series heteroscedasticity.
3:85:4934:22:22:89:539:219:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Our model has three assumptions.  First, firms’ growth rates are influenced by the growth
3:86:5023:22:21:72:540:197:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:rates of their customers.  As a result, the firm-specific shocks propagate through the network
3:87:5117:21:22:72:540:176:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:via connected firms.  Second, the probability of a customer-supplier link depends on the size
3:88:5210:22:22:72:540:154:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of the supplier so that large firms typically supply to a higher number of customers.  Third,
3:89:5303:22:21:72:540:132:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the  importance  of  a  customer-supplier  link  depends  on  the  size  of  the  customer.   Large
3:90:5402:21:18:72:540:111:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:customers have a stronger connection with their suppliers, presumably because they account
3:91:5492:18:16:85:539:93:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:1See also Leahy and Whited (1996), Bloom, Bond, and Van Reenen (2007), Stokey (2012) and the papers
3:92:5591:16:0:72:130:77:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:cited therein.
4:93:5606:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for a large fraction of their suppliers’ sales.  We provide microeconomic evidence for all three
4:94:5702:22:22:72:526:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:assumptions based on the observed customer-supplier networks among Compustat firms.
4:95:5785:22:21:89:539:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In our model, firms are aggregators of their own idiosyncratic shocks and the shocks to
4:96:5872:21:22:72:540:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:connected firms.  The sparsity and granularity of a firm’s customer network, which in turn
4:97:5962:22:22:72:540:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:depend on the firm size distribution, determine how well a firm diversifies the shocks that it
4:98:6056:22:21:72:540:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is exposed to and thus determine its volatility.  By connecting the firm size distribution to
4:99:6149:21:22:72:540:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:network formation, our model generates a rich set of implications for volatility in the cross
4:100:6242:22:22:72:540:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:section and the time series, which we can test.  We study data on firm-level sizes, volatilities,
4:101:6339:22:21:72:540:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and customer-supplier linkages, establishing a new set of stylized facts about firm volatility
4:102:6433:21:22:72:281:513:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and confirming the model’s implications.
4:103:6473:22:22:89:539:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Firm-level  volatilities  exhibit  a  common  factor  structure  where  the  factor  is  firm  size
4:104:6572:22:21:72:540:469:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dispersion in the economy.  In the model, each supplier’s network is a random draw from the
4:105:6663:21:22:72:540:448:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:entire population of firms, so that any firm’s customer network inherits similar dispersion
4:106:6754:22:22:72:540:426:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to  that  of  the  entire  size  distribution.   An  increase  in  dispersion  slows  down  every  firm’s
4:107:6859:22:21:72:540:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shock diversification and increases their volatility.  In the data, we find that firm volatilities
4:108:6957:21:22:72:540:383:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:possess  a  strong  factor  structure,  and  we  show  that  size  dispersion  explains  25%  of  the
4:109:7058:22:22:72:540:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:variation in (realized) firm volatilities, as much as is explained by average volatility, a natural
4:110:7157:22:21:72:135:339:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:benchmark.2
4:111:7168:21:22:89:539:318:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The factor structure implies strong time series correlations between moments of the size
4:112:7256:22:22:72:540:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and volatility distributions.  An increase in the size dispersion translates into higher average
4:113:7352:22:21:72:540:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility  among  firms.   It  also  raises  the  cross  section  dispersion  in  volatilities.   In  the
4:114:7458:21:22:72:540:253:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:time series, size dispersion has a 72% correlation with mean firm volatility and 79% with the
4:115:7551:22:22:72:540:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dispersion of firm volatility.  Our paper is the first to provide an economic explanation for the
4:116:7648:22:22:72:540:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:factor structure in firm-level volatility by connecting it to firm concentration.  A persistent
4:117:7743:22:21:72:540:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:widening in the firm size dispersion should lead to a persistent rise in mean firm volatility.
4:118:7837:21:22:72:540:166:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We observe such a widening (increase in firm concentration) between the early 1960s and
4:119:7924:22:18:72:540:144:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the late 1990s, providing a new explanation for the trend in mean firm volatility studied by
4:120:8016:18:15:85:539:126:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:2Similarly,  Engle  and  Figlewski  (2012)  document  a  common  factor  in  option-implied  volatilities  since
4:121:8128:15:12:72:540:111:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:1996,  and  Barigozzi,  Brownlees,  Gallo,  and  Veredas  (2010)  and  Veredas  and  Luciani  (2012)  examine  the
4:122:8242:12:12:72:540:99:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:factor structure in realized volatilities of intra-daily returns since 2001.  Bloom, Floetotto, Jaimovich, and
4:123:8352:12:12:72:540:87:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Terry  (2012)  shows  that  firm-specific  sales  growth  and  productivity  show  cross-sectional  dispersion  that
4:124:8468:12:0:72:224:75:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:fluctuates with the macroeconomy.
5:125:8502:0:22:72:192:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Campbell et al.  (2001).
5:126:8526:22:22:89:539:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In the cross-section, differences in volatility across firms arise from two sources: differences
5:127:8622:22:21:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the number of a customers and differences in customer size dispersion. First, large firms are
5:128:8718:21:22:72:540:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:less volatile than small firms because they are connected to more customers, which improves
5:129:8809:22:22:72:540:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:diversification  regardless  of  the  size  profile  of  its  customer  base.   This  effect  also  appears
5:130:8916:22:21:72:540:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the volatility factor structure in our model.  Smaller firms have larger exposures to the
5:131:9008:21:22:72:540:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:common volatility factor, implying that small firms have both higher levels of volatility and
5:132:9101:22:22:72:540:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:higher volatility of volatility.  In the data, doubling a firm’s size lowers its volatility by 32%,
5:133:9200:22:21:72:530:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and small firms indeed have higher volatility factor exposures, as predicted by the model.
5:134:9290:21:22:89:539:513:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Second, holding the number of connections fixed, a supplier’s customer network is less
5:135:9376:22:22:72:540:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:diversified  if  there  is  more  dispersion  in  the  size  of  its  customers.   Because  customer  size
5:136:9482:22:21:72:540:469:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:determines the strength of a link, severe customer size disparity means that shocks to the
5:137:9572:21:22:72:540:448:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:biggest  customers  exert  an  outsized  influence  on  the  supplier,  which  raises  the  supplier’s
5:138:9674:22:22:72:540:426:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility.  Differences in customer size disparity arise from probabilistic network formation
5:139:9768:22:21:72:540:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:– some suppliers will link to a very large (or very small) customer by chance alone.  The
5:140:9857:21:22:72:540:383:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data indeed show a strong negative cross-sectional relationship between firm size and firm
5:141:9947:22:22:72:540:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility and a strong positive correlation between a firm’s “out-Herfindahl,” our measure
5:142:10038:22:21:72:540:339:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of concentration in a firm’s customer network,  and its volatility.  The dependence of firm
5:143:10129:21:22:72:540:318:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility on firm size and firm out-Herfindahl survives the inclusion of other determinants
5:144:10221:22:22:72:540:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of volatility previously proposed in the empirical literature, including industry concentration
5:145:10316:22:21:72:540:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and competition, R&D intensity, equity ownership composition, firm age, and cohort effects.
5:146:10407:21:22:72:492:253:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Collectively, this evidence supports a network-based explanation of firm volatility.
5:147:10491:22:22:89:539:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:A calibrated version of our model matches the data not only qualitatively but also quan-
5:148:10579:22:22:72:540:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:titatively.  We target moments and cross-moments of data on firm size, firm volatility, and
5:149:10670:22:21:72:540:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:inter-firm business linkages.3A benchmark calibration with strong network effects is able to
5:150:10762:21:22:72:540:166:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:account quantitatively for most cross-sectional and aggregate features of the size, volatility,
5:151:10857:22:18:72:540:144:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and  linkage  distributions.   But  it  overstates  the  degree  of  concentration  in  customer  net-
5:152:10959:18:15:85:539:126:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:3While  we  are  able  to  analytically  characterize  volatility  behavior  in  the  large-firm  limit,  ascertaining
5:153:11077:15:12:72:540:111:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:certain  other  model  features  necessitates  a  numerical  simulation  due  to  the  inherent  non-linearity  of  the
5:154:11196:12:12:72:540:99:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:network  and  its  dynamics  (exit  and  entry  of  firms  and  persistence  in  connections).   The  calibration  also
5:155:11315:12:0:72:388:87:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:allows us to confront truncation and selection issues we face in the data.
6:156:11390:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:works and underestimates the cross-sectional dispersion in volatility.  An extension of the
6:157:11481:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model that allows for internal diversification, for example from operating multiple product
6:158:11572:22:21:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lines, is able to match the observed dispersion in volatility and degree of customer network
6:159:11664:21:22:72:540:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:concentration, while continuing to match the other size and volatility moments.  We show
6:160:11752:22:22:72:540:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that to match the joint distribution of volatility and size, we need both internal and external
6:161:11847:22:21:72:268:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(network) diversification in the model.
6:162:11886:21:22:89:539:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The  model  displays  substantial  movement  in  aggregate  moments  of  size  and  volatility
6:163:11980:22:22:72:540:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:distributions, despite allowing for thousands of firms, because the granular network intro-
6:164:12071:22:21:72:540:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:duces strong feedback between the cross-sectional size distribution and firm-level volatility:
6:165:12165:21:22:72:540:513:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:even small shocks to size dispersion have large dynamic volatility effects.  The data display
6:166:12258:22:22:72:540:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:similarly  large  fluctuations  as  in  our  model.   Without  network  effects,  our  model  reduces
6:167:12359:22:21:72:540:469:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to a Gibrat model and implies that the cross-sectional size and volatility distributions are
6:168:12451:21:22:72:225:448:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:effectively constant over time.
6:169:12482:22:22:89:539:426:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We build upon a rich literature exploring the role of input-output network linkages for
6:170:12569:22:21:72:540:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:aggregate fluctuations, exemplified by Long and Plosser (1987), Carvalho (2010), Foerster,
6:171:12659:21:22:72:540:383:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Sarte, and Watson (2011), Acemoglu et al. (2012), and Carvalho and Gabaix (2013).  Our
6:172:12745:22:22:72:540:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model ties the size distribution to network formation, and shows that network connectivity
6:173:12835:22:21:72:540:339:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is crucial for describing not only aggregate volatility, but the entire distribution of firm-level
6:174:12933:21:22:72:540:318:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility.  We combine the network sparsity insights of Acemoglu et al. (2012) with Gabaix’s
6:175:13026:22:22:72:540:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(2011) notion of limited diversification through granularity.  The granularity of the network
6:176:13119:22:21:72:540:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is related to the idea that a few large firms account for a large part of aggregate output and
6:177:13213:21:22:72:540:253:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:hence of aggregate volatility.  Our granularity, by operating at the firm level, endogenously
6:178:13306:22:22:72:540:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:generates firm-level volatilities rather than taking those as given.  While consistent with the
6:179:13401:22:22:72:540:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:economic structure in these papers, our model differs from earlier work by directly specifying
6:180:13495:22:21:72:540:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the statistical mechanics of inter-firm linkages.  This approach allows us to derive tractable
6:181:13589:21:22:72:540:166:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:analytic statements about firm volatility in large economies while still allowing for rich size
6:182:13684:22:22:72:190:144:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and network dynamics.
6:183:13705:22:21:89:539:122:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The rest of the paper is organized as follows.  Section 2 sets up the network model and
6:184:13792:21:22:72:540:101:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:studies its volatility implications.  It also justifies the modeling assumptions based on micro
6:185:13887:22:0:72:540:79:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data on firms’ networks.  Section 3 presents macro evidence on the link between the firm size
7:186:13981:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and the firm volatility distributions.  Section 4 calibrates the model and studies its ability to
7:187:14078:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:match the size, volatility, and network data.  The proofs of the theoretical results, auxiliary
7:188:14173:22:51:72:501:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:empirical evidence, and additional calibration results are relegated to the appendix.
7:189:14258:51:33:72:518:613:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:2    A Network Model of Firm Growth and Volatility
7:190:14308:33:22:72:540:580:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We  develop  a  dynamic  network  model  of  connections  between  firms.   While  the  model  is
7:191:14405:22:22:72:540:558:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:more  general,  our  empirical  application  leads  us  to  interpret  the  network  as  one  between
7:192:14506:22:43:72:198:536:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:suppliers and customers.
7:193:14530:43:30:72:203:493:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:2.1    Firm Growth
7:194:14548:30:39:72:397:463:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:DefineSi,tas the size of firmiwith growth rate asgi,t+1, where
7:195:14610:39:40:248:540:424:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:A:Si,t+1=Si,texp(gi,t+1).(1)
7:196:14636:40:21:72:539:384:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this directed network, supplieri’s growth rates depend on its own idiosyncratic shock and
7:197:14728:21:43:72:366:363:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a weighted average of the growth rates of its customersj:

7:204:15006:22:22:72:539:232:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:growth rate of firmj.  Ifiandjare not connected thenwi,j,t= 0.  By convention, we set

7:208:15350:19:16:85:539:148:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:4As an aside, these growth dynamics impute dynamics to the relative sizes, or shares, of firms that are
7:209:15453:16:12:72:540:132:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:similar to those explored by Menzly, Santos, and Veronesi (2004) and Santos and Veronesi (2006).  In contrast
7:210:15562:12:12:72:540:120:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:to their work, there is no mean reversion built into our shares.  Furthermore, only the customer shares in
7:211:15668:12:12:72:540:108:10:g_d0_f10:10:g_d0_f5:F:Wtare relevant for the cash flow dynamics of a firm.  These shares divide by the sum of all customers not
7:212:15773:12:0:72:243:96:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:by the sum of all firms in the economy.
8:213:15813:0:40:72:429:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:be theN×1 vectors of growth rates and shocks, respectively, so that
8:214:15880:40:0:157:386:668:12:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f3:C:gt+1=μg+γWtgt+1+εt+1= (I−γWt)−1
8:215:15911:0:10:389:394:678:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:(

8:218:15920:10:40:450:540:668:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:A:.(3)
8:219:15924:40:21:89:539:628:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We  purposely  impose  stark  assumptions  on  the  nature  of  the  underlying  innovations:
8:220:16017:21:22:72:539:607:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:each firmiexperiences i.i.d. growth rate shocksεi,t+1∼N(0,σε).  Under this assumption,
8:221:16103:22:22:72:539:585:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:all dynamics in the volatility of growth rates arise endogenously.5In Section 4 we consider
8:222:16194:22:21:72:540:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a model extension that allows for heterogeneity in firms’ shock volatilities and find the same
8:223:16288:21:22:72:363:542:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:qualitative effects of network structure on firm volatility.
8:224:16348:22:22:89:539:520:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Acemoglu  et  al.  (2012)  derive  a  static  version  of  (3)  as  the  equilibrium  outcome  in  a
8:225:16448:22:21:72:539:498:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:multi-sector production economy.6Theirs is also a directed network, but in their version the
8:226:16540:21:22:71:539:477:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:productivity shocks are transferred downstream from suppliers to customers.7The appendix
8:227:16628:22:22:71:539:455:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:explores a version of our model where shocks are propagated downstream from suppliers to
8:228:16716:22:21:71:539:433:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:customers.  In Section 4, we argue that the data are more supportive of an upstream than a
8:229:16806:21:44:71:298:412:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:downstream shock propagation mechanism.8
8:230:16846:44:29:71:345:368:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:2.2    Size Effects in Network Structure
8:231:16886:29:22:71:539:339:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:So far, the statistical model for firm growth is a general representation of a spatial autore-
8:232:16980:22:19:71:539:317:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:gression.  The content of the network (spatial autoregression) model is in the specification of
8:233:17075:19:15:85:539:298:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:5As  in  a  Gibrat  model,  the  model  described  here  is  non-stationary.   Our  theoretical  analysis  of  firm
8:234:17190:15:12:72:540:283:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:volatility is unaffected by this property.  In the calibration of Section 4, which includes time series moments,
8:235:17302:12:12:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:we introduce firm entry and exit into the model to maintain stationarity.  This is a standard mechanism used
8:236:17410:12:8:72:291:259:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:in models of the size distribution (de Wit (2005)).
8:237:17461:8:16:85:539:251:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:6Theirs  is  a  constant-returns  to  scale  economy  populated  by  a  stand-in  agent  who  has  Cobb-Douglas
8:238:17572:16:12:72:540:235:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:preferences defined over all of theNdifferent commodities produced.  Productivity shocks are transferred
8:239:17676:12:12:72:539:223:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f13:F:downstream from suppliers to customers.  In this economy, the weightswk,jare the input weights of goodk
8:240:17779:12:12:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:for firmjin the production of commodityjwhileγmeasures the intermediate goods share. This specification
8:241:17882:12:12:72:540:199:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:emphasizes upstream supply shocks as the only drivers of firm-level volatility, while our model focuses on
8:242:17988:12:8:72:254:187:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:downstream demand shocks as the driver.
8:243:18027:8:16:85:539:179:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:7Since  we  focus  on  propagation  from  customer  to  supplier,  a  question  arises  about  retail  firms  whose
8:244:18142:16:12:72:540:163:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:customers are households.  Because households are not part of the model, this would appear to be a source of
8:245:18250:12:12:72:540:151:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:leakage.  In the data, we also consider retailers who do not have any customer connections with other firms,
8:246:18358:12:12:72:540:139:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:at least not in the standard sense.  Our simple model can be extended to include retailers.  If markets are
8:247:18465:12:11:72:540:127:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:incomplete, then some of the labor income risk that is specific to firms would show up in the consumption
8:248:18570:11:9:72:493:116:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:decisions of workers at these firms, which in turn would expose retail firms to the upstream risk.
8:249:18668:9:15:85:539:107:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:8Shock transmission in the economy is plausibly bi-directional.  We focus on uni-directional transmission
8:250:18773:15:12:72:540:92:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:to clearly highlight the network and size mechanisms of interest.  Our setup is easily generalized, but at the
8:251:18883:12:0:72:192:80:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:cost of analytic tractability.
9:252:18914:0:22:72:539:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the weighting matrixW.  We make two key assumptions on the probability and the strength
9:253:19001:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of connections between customers and suppliers.  These assumptions connect the firm size
9:254:19089:22:21:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:distribution to the network structure of the economy.  We provide micro-economic evidence
9:255:19178:21:22:72:259:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for these assumptions in Section 2.6.
9:256:19215:22:22:89:540:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The linkage structure at timetis determined by the firm size distribution coming into
9:257:19300:22:50:72:399:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:periodt.  The existence of a link betweeniandjis captured by
9:258:19360:50:0:202:234:549:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:A:bi,j,t=
9:259:19367:0:11:237:248:577:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:

9:264:19416:36:22:71:539:498:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Each element of the connections matrix,Bt= [bi,j,t], is drawn from a Bernoulli distribution
9:265:19507:22:41:72:512:476:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:withP(bi,j,t= 1).  This connection probability is assumed to be linear in supplier size:

9:271:19731:21:22:72:540:374:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is  a  scale  constant.   While  the  precise  functional  form  matters  quantitatively,  the  crucial

9:273:19927:22:21:72:540:330:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of the supplier, not on the size of the customer.  It follows immediately that larger suppliers

9:275:20096:22:22:89:539:287:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Equation (4)  also  builds sparsity  into  the  network.  The sparsity  parameterζ∈(0,1)

9:279:20468:22:41:72:320:200:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:expected number of customers (or out-degree) is

9:284:20541:39:22:71:539:120:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The  number  of  linkages  grows  with  the  number  of  firms  in  the  economy,  but  the  rate  of
9:285:20642:22:18:71:266:98:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:growth is slower whenζis closer to 1.
9:286:20679:18:0:85:430:80:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:9Empirical support of this assumption is discussed in Sections 2.6 and 4 below.

11:347:22872:22:21:72:539:398:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility  of  the  underlying  innovationsσ2ε,  and  the  strength  of  shock  transmission  in  the

11:349:22983:22:22:89:540:355:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The  higheri’s  out-Herfindahl,  the  more  concentrated  is  its  customer  network  and  the
11:350:23077:22:21:72:540:333:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:higher  is  its  variance.   A  supplier’s  out-Herfindahl  (and  hence  firm  variance)  is  driven  by
11:351:23181:21:22:72:540:312:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:two features, the number of customers it has and the amount of dispersion in its customers’
11:352:23272:22:22:72:540:290:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sizes.  The supplier is more diversified when it has more customers and when the dispersion
11:353:23363:22:21:72:540:268:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in customer sizes is small.  Because all firms, large and small, draw their connections from the
11:354:23459:21:22:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:same economy-wide size distribution, their customer networks have equal size dispersion in
11:355:23549:22:22:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:expectation.  In our model, the key difference in the network structure across suppliers is the
11:356:23644:22:21:72:539:203:12:g_d0_f15:12:g_d0_f2:B:numberof customers they have, which depends on the supplier’s size through the Bernoulli
11:357:23732:21:22:72:540:182:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:probability function.  This commonality in network concentration naturally leads to a factor
11:358:23824:22:22:72:540:160:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:structure in firm volatilities, where the factor is the dispersion of the economy-wide firm size
11:359:23920:22:21:72:135:138:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:distribution.

13:439:26391:22:21:71:539:447:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:economy.  First, firm variance depends on economy-wide firm size dispersionE[S2]/E[S]2,

13:441:26574:22:22:71:539:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:since more size dispersion makes every supplier less diversified,  and this has ripple effects
13:442:26668:22:21:71:539:382:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:through the higher order terms (customers’ customer networks are also less diversified, and
13:443:26759:21:22:71:525:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:so on).  Asγapproaches one, these higher order terms become quantitatively important.
13:444:26844:22:22:89:539:339:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Relative firm size, ̃Si, appears in two terms.  Its primary role is in the first coefficient on
13:445:26939:22:21:71:539:317:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:B:E[S2]/E[S]2.  Intuitively, larger firms have a lower exposure to the common size dispersion
13:446:27030:21:22:71:539:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:factor than do smaller firms because they typically connect to more customers, achieve better
13:447:27123:22:22:71:539:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shock diversification, and display lower volatility.  Smaller factor exposure also makes large
13:448:27217:22:21:71:539:252:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:firms  less  sensitive  to  fluctuations  in  the  firm  size  dispersion;  that  is,  they  display  lower
13:449:27324:21:22:71:182:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility of volatility.
13:450:27349:22:22:89:539:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The firm’s relative size also appears in the numerator of the last term.  Shocks to the
13:451:27436:22:21:71:539:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:largest firms are the most strongly propagated shocks in the model since these firms have the
13:452:27529:21:22:71:539:166:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:largest influence on their suppliers.  These shocks feed back into large firms’ own volatility
13:453:27623:22:22:71:539:144:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:while, in contrast, small firms’ shocks die out relatively quickly in the network.  Thus, the
13:454:27716:22:21:71:539:122:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:last term in Proposition 1 captures a countervailing increase in volatility for larger firms.  In
13:455:27813:21:22:71:496:101:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:all of our numerical results, we find that the effect of the first size term dominates.
13:456:27900:22:0:89:540:79:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Finally, the variance of firm variance decays at rateN1−ζ, showing the dominance ofHi’s
14:457:27989:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:effect on total volatility.  The dependence of volatility’s decay rate onζcaptures the effect of
14:458:28085:22:22:72:539:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:network sparsity.  Highζmeans that there are relatively few linkages compared to the size
14:459:28174:22:21:72:404:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of the economy, which slows down the diversification of all firms.
14:460:28240:21:22:89:539:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Thus far we have assumed that the firm size distribution has finite variance, thus the slow
14:461:28331:22:22:72:540:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility decay in Proposition 1 arises only due to network sparsity.  Gabaix (2011) empha-
14:462:28423:22:21:72:540:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sizes that extreme right skewness of firm sizes can also slow down volatility decay in large
14:463:28515:21:22:72:540:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:economies.  In the next result, we show that the firm-level network structure adds a mecha-
14:464:28606:22:22:72:540:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:nism to further slow down volatility decay beyond Gabaix’s (2011) granularity mechanism,
14:465:28694:22:31:72:409:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:which depends on the power law behavior of the size distribution.
14:466:28759:31:21:72:540:503:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f15:C:Proposition 2.Consider  a  sequence  of  economies  indexed  by  the  number  of  firmsN.   If
14:467:28853:21:22:72:539:482:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f15:C:Siis  distributed  as  a  power  law  with  exponentη∈(1,2],  then  firm  variance  decays  at  rate
14:468:28953:22:31:72:139:460:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f15:C:N(1−ζ)(2−2/η).
14:469:28967:31:22:89:539:429:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In the absence of network effects, power law sizes would imply that firm variance decays
14:470:29055:22:21:72:539:407:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:at rateN2−2/η.  For any given rate of decay determined by the power law, network sparsity
14:471:29144:21:22:72:248:386:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:further lowers the decay rate byζ.
14:472:29178:22:22:89:539:364:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We have referred to the volatility structure described in Proposition 1 as a factor model.
14:473:29268:22:21:72:539:342:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The  next  result  characterizes  comovement  among  firms’  volatilities  whenNis  large.   Be-
14:474:29364:21:22:72:539:321:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:causeHidetermines the rate of convergence for firm variance, we may understand how firm
14:475:29451:22:22:72:540:299:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:variances covary in a large economy by studying the asymptotic covariance amongHiand
14:476:29535:22:30:72:89:277:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:B:Hj.
14:477:29538:30:22:72:539:247:12:g_d0_f1:8:g_d0_f7:C:Proposition 3.Consider a sequence of economies indexed by the number of firmsN.  IfSi
14:478:29623:22:22:72:539:225:12:g_d0_f15:12:g_d0_f15:C:has  finite  fourth  moment  (e.g.,  ifSiis  a  lognormal  variable),  then  the  covariance  between
14:479:29724:22:39:72:301:203:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f15:C:HiandHjhas limiting behavior described by
14:480:29765:39:0:207:282:164:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f9:C:Cov(Hi,Hj)∼
14:481:29776:0:17:308:314:172:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:1
14:482:29777:17:0:287:334:155:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f3:C:N1+2(1−ζ)
14:483:29786:0:17:337:369:172:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:A:V(S2)
14:484:29791:17:0:342:362:155:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:C:SiSj
14:485:29795:0:17:378:391:172:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f3:C:Z2
14:486:29797:17:0:371:398:155:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f3:C:E[S]2
14:487:29802:0:40:400:403:164:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:C:.
14:488:29803:40:31:72:406:124:12:g_d0_f15:12:g_d0_f15:C:The covariance betweenV(gi)andV(gj)decays at the same rate.
14:489:29862:31:21:89:539:93:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:As the number of firms grows, not only does the level of volatility decay, but so does its
14:490:29952:21:0:72:540:72:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:variance and covariance between the volatilities of different firms.  Proposition 3 shows that
15:491:30048:0:22:72:539:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:comovement among firm variances decays at rateN1+2(1−ζ).  Intuitively, covariance is lowest
15:492:30139:22:44:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:when both firms are large, since large firms have low exposure to overall size concentration.
15:493:30232:44:29:72:251:642:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:2.4    Aggregate Volatility
15:494:30259:29:22:72:539:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f6:B:We next characterize the behavior of aggregate volatility.  LetStbe the vector of sizes,ι
15:495:30348:22:21:71:539:591:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:anN-vector of ones, andνt=St/ι′Stthe vector of size weights.  We define the aggregate
15:496:30433:21:40:72:227:570:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:growth rate of the economy as
15:497:30462:40:0:186:357:530:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f3:C:ga,t+1=ν′tgt+1=ν′t(I−γWt)−1
15:498:30489:0:10:360:365:540:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:(
15:499:30490:10:0:365:414:530:12:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f3:C:μg+εt+1
15:500:30497:0:10:414:419:540:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:)
15:501:30498:10:40:421:424:530:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:C:.
15:502:30499:40:39:72:346:490:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The variance of the aggregate growth rate is given by
15:503:30552:39:40:185:425:451:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:C:Vt(ga,t+1) =σ2εν′t(I−γWt)−1(I−γW′t)−1νt.
15:504:30592:40:22:71:540:411:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:A first observation is that variation inWtandStinduces heteroscedasticity in aggregate
15:505:30678:22:21:72:540:389:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:growth rates, even though all underlying innovations are i.i.d. across firms and over time.
15:506:30769:21:22:72:540:368:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The  next  proposition  shows  that  our  network  model  drives  a  wedge  between  the  rates  of
15:507:30868:22:32:72:307:346:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:decay for aggregate versus firm-level variance.
15:508:30915:32:21:72:539:314:12:g_d0_f1:8:g_d0_f7:C:Proposition 4.Consider a sequence of economies indexed by the number of firmsN.  IfSi
15:509:31000:21:40:71:443:293:12:g_d0_f15:12:g_d0_f15:C:has finite variance, then firm volatility has limiting behavior described by
15:510:31076:40:0:230:289:253:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f9:C:Vt(ga,t+1)∼
15:511:31087:0:16:308:319:261:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:C:σ2ε
15:512:31090:16:0:294:334:245:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:A:(1−γ)2
15:513:31096:0:16:342:371:261:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:A:E[S2]
15:514:31101:16:0:337:376:245:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f3:C:NE[S]2
15:515:31107:0:39:377:380:253:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:C:.
15:516:31108:39:22:72:539:214:12:g_d0_f15:12:g_d0_f15:C:IfSiis  distributed  as  a  power  law  with  exponentη∈(1,2],  then  aggregate  variance  decays
15:517:31205:22:32:71:148:192:12:g_d0_f15:12:g_d0_f15:C:at rateN2−2/η.
15:518:31219:32:21:89:540:160:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:When the variance of firm size is finite,  aggregate variance decays at rate 1/N,  which
15:519:31307:21:22:72:540:139:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is  generally  faster  than  the  rate  of  decay  of  firm  variance.   That  is,  even  if  the  firm  size
15:520:31416:22:22:72:540:117:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:distribution  is  lognormal,  there  is  slow  volatility  decay  at  the  firm  level  but  not  at  the
15:521:31521:22:0:72:540:95:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:aggregate  level.   For  size  distributions  with  infinite  variance  (but  finite  mean),  aggregate
16:522:31626:0:44:72:430:708:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:variance decays more slowly than 1/N, but remains unaffected byζ.10
16:523:31693:44:29:72:240:664:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:2.5    Residual Volatility
16:524:31719:29:22:72:540:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In the literature,  idiosyncratic volatility is typically constructed by first removing the ag-
16:525:31814:22:22:72:540:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:gregate component of growth rates with a statistical procedure such as principal component
16:526:31904:22:21:72:540:591:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:analysis, then calculating the volatilities of the residuals.  In a granular network model like
16:527:31999:21:22:72:540:570:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ours, such a factor regression approach is misspecified.  There is no dimension-reducing com-
16:528:32092:22:22:72:540:548:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f15:B:mon factor that fully captures growth rate comovement since, by virtue of the network,every
16:529:32183:22:21:72:540:526:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:firm’s shock may be systematic.  A sign of the misspecification of the factor model is that the
16:530:32278:21:22:72:540:505:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:residuals exhibit a volatility factor structure that looks very similar to the factor structure
16:531:32373:22:22:72:540:483:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for total firm volatility.  In our empirical analysis below we show that the key features of
16:532:32465:22:43:72:431:461:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:total volatilities also exist for volatilities of factor regression residuals.
16:533:32543:43:30:72:324:418:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:2.6    Justifying Model Assumptions
16:534:32578:30:22:72:540:388:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Before we turn to the empirical relationship between the firm size and firm volatility distri-
16:535:32672:22:21:72:540:366:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:bution,  we discuss how customer-supplier network micro-data support our main modeling
16:536:32758:21:22:72:138:345:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:assumptions.
16:537:32770:22:22:89:539:323:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Our data for annual firm-level linkages come from Compustat.  It includes the fraction of
16:538:32859:22:21:72:540:301:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a firm’s dollar sales to each of its major customers.  Firms are required to supply customer
16:539:32951:21:22:72:540:280:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:information in accordance with Financial Accounting Standards Rule No. 131, in which a
16:540:33037:22:22:72:540:258:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:major customer is defined as any firm that is responsible for more than 10% of the reporting
16:541:33129:22:21:72:540:236:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:seller’s revenue.11The Compustat data have been carefully linked to CRSP market equity
16:542:33215:21:22:72:540:215:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data by Cohen and Frazzini (2008), which allows us to associate information on firms’ net-
16:543:33305:22:18:72:539:193:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:work connectivity with their market equity value and return volatility.12The data set covers
16:544:33397:18:15:81:539:175:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:10As shown by Acemoglu et al. (2012), network sparsity may impact aggregate volatility.  Our model, by
16:545:33499:15:12:72:540:160:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:focusing on size as the key determinant of network formation, combines the aggregate volatility effects of
16:546:33605:12:9:72:525:148:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:network sparsity and granularity into a single mechanism that acts through the power law parameterη.
16:547:33705:9:15:81:539:139:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:11Firms have discretion in reporting relationships with customers that account for less than 10% of their
16:548:33810:15:12:71:539:124:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:sales, and this is occasionally observed.  In our data, 23% of firms report at least one customer that accounts
16:549:33921:12:8:71:196:112:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:for less that 10% of its sales.
16:550:33952:8:16:81:539:104:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:12Cohen  and  Frazzini  (2008)  used  the  same  data  to  show  that  news  about  business  partners  does  not
16:551:34065:16:12:71:539:88:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:immediately get reflected into stock prices.  Atalay et al. (2011) also use Compustat sales linkage data to
16:552:34172:12:0:71:278:76:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:develop a model of customer-supplier networks.
17:553:34220:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the period 1980-2009,  and includes 48,839 customer-supplier-year observations.  Appendix
17:554:34309:22:22:72:436:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:B.1 provides more details and summary statistics on the network data.
17:555:34378:22:21:89:539:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The first size assumption in the model is that suppliers have stronger connections with
17:556:34465:21:22:72:540:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:their larger customers.  This is a prominent feature of the data.  Specifically, for each supplier
17:557:34563:22:22:72:540:621:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:B:iin the Compustat data, we calculate the correlation between its customers’ total sales (Sj,t)
17:558:34657:22:21:72:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and the fraction ofi’s revenue that is due to each customer (wi,j,t).  We then average across all
17:559:34754:21:22:72:539:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:suppliers in a given year, and finally average across all years.  We also compute at-statistic
17:560:34848:22:22:72:540:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:equal  to  the  ratio  of  the  time  series  mean  of  the  correlation  to  its  time-series  standard
17:561:34952:22:21:72:540:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:deviation.   In  an  average  year,  that  correlation  is  20%  (t=  3.6).   The  correlation  is  19%
17:562:35055:21:22:72:413:513:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:when we limit ourselves to only use links that exceed 10% of sales.
17:563:35122:22:22:89:539:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Our  second  size  assumption  is  that  larger  suppliers  are  connected  to  more  customers
17:564:35217:22:21:72:540:469:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on  average  (higherNi).   The  Compustat  data  cannot  speak  directly  to  this  assumption
17:565:35311:21:22:72:539:448:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:due to the truncation of all linkages whose weights fall below 10%.13To address this,  we
17:566:35400:22:22:72:540:426:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:simulate a calibrated version of our model and estimate the correlation between supplier size
17:567:35493:22:21:72:540:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and out-degree in both the full simulated sample and the simulated sample while imposing
17:568:35581:21:22:72:540:383:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the  10%  weight  truncation.   We  find  that  truncation  produces  a  downward  bias  in  the
17:569:35677:22:22:72:540:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlation estimate of 19 to 27 percentage points depending on the calibration.  Since the
17:570:35768:22:21:72:540:339:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:measured correlation in the actual (truncated) data is 3%, the bias-adjusted estimate is 22%
17:571:35860:21:22:72:540:318:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to 30% based on our model.  The data also support our assumption that the probability of
17:572:35948:22:22:72:540:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a connection is independent of customer size (the truncation-adjusted correlation between
17:573:36037:22:21:72:540:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:customer size and number of suppliers is 4% in the data).  We discuss these points further
17:574:36127:21:22:72:136:253:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in Section 4.
17:575:36140:22:22:89:539:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Inter-industry trade data from the Bureau of Economic Analysis do not suffer truncation,
17:576:36228:22:22:72:539:209:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:so for comparison we test the correlation between size and out-degree at the industry level.14
17:577:36322:22:21:72:540:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We find a 61% correlation between the size of a supplier industry and the number of industries
17:578:36416:21:22:72:539:166:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f7:B:that it sells to.  We also find a 37% correlation between the size of the customer industrySj,t
17:579:36511:22:19:72:238:144:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and importance of the linkwi,j,t.
17:580:36544:19:15:81:539:125:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:13For example, a small firm that has a single customer accounting for 100% of its revenue can show up as
17:581:36648:15:9:72:540:110:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:having more links than a firm with 11 equally important customers with weights of 9.1% due to truncation.
17:582:36753:9:15:81:539:101:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:14We use the BEA’s input-output tables for the 65-industry breakdown of the US economy between 1998
17:583:36852:15:0:72:114:86:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:and 2011.
18:584:36863:0:34:72:523:708:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:3    Macro Evidence on Size Dispersion and Volatility
18:585:36916:34:22:72:540:674:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This section documents new stylized facts about the joint evolution of the firm size and firm
18:586:37009:22:43:72:189:652:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility distributions.
18:587:37034:43:30:72:143:609:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:3.1    Data
18:588:37045:30:21:72:540:579:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We  conduct  our  analysis  using  stock  market  data  from  CRSP  over  the  period  1926–2010
18:589:37141:21:22:72:540:558:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and using cash flow data from CRSP/Compustat over 1952–2010.  We consider market and
18:590:37225:22:22:72:540:536:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fundamental  measures  of  firm  size  and  firm  volatility  calculated  at  the  annual  frequency.
18:591:37326:22:21:72:540:514:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:For size,  we use equity market value at the end of the calendar year or total sales within
18:592:37417:21:22:72:540:493:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the  calendar  year.15Market  volatility  is  defined  as  the  standard  deviation  of  daily  stock
18:593:37518:22:22:72:540:471:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:returns during the calendar year.  Fundamental volatility in yeartis defined as the standard
18:594:37610:22:21:72:540:449:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:deviation of quarterly sales growth (over the same quarter the previous year) within calendar
18:595:37703:21:22:72:160:428:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:yearsttot+ 4.16
18:596:37718:22:22:89:540:406:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In the data, size and volatility are well approximated by a lognormal distribution.17As a
18:597:37807:22:21:72:540:384:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:result, the dynamics of each distribution may be summarized with two moments:  the cross
18:598:37895:21:22:72:540:363:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:section mean and standard deviation of the log quantities.  We now examine the dynamics
18:599:37982:22:44:72:211:341:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of these moments in detail.
18:600:38009:44:29:72:452:297:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:3.2    Comovement of Size and Volatility Distributions
18:601:38063:29:22:72:539:268:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Figure 1 plots the cross-sectionalaverageof log firm variance against lagged firm size dis-
18:602:38154:22:21:72:540:246:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:persion, where size is based on market capitalization and volatility is based on stock returns.
18:603:38249:21:22:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The correlation between average volatility and the market-based measure of size dispersion is
18:604:38342:22:22:72:540:203:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:71.7%.  Mean firm volatility experienced several large swings in the past century, especially
18:605:38435:22:21:72:540:181:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in  the  1920s  and  1930s  and  again  in  the  last  two  decades  of  the  sample.   These  changes
18:606:38537:21:18:72:540:160:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are preceded by similar dynamics in the cross-sectional dispersion of firm size.  The close
18:607:38628:18:12:81:394:142:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:15All variables in our analysis are deflated by the consumer price index.
18:608:38701:12:16:81:539:130:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:16We also consider fundamental volatility measured by the standard deviation of quarterly sales growth
18:609:38803:16:12:72:540:114:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:within  a  single  calendar  year.   The  one-  and  five-year  fundamental  volatility  estimates  are  qualitatively
18:610:38921:12:8:72:453:102:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:identical, though the one-year measure is noisier because it uses only four observations.
18:611:39010:8:0:81:498:94:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:17Detailed distributional statistics for the CRSP/Compustat sample are available upon request.

19:629:39584:46:22:72:509:396:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:association between mean volatility and size dispersion is predicted by Proposition 1.
19:630:39670:22:22:89:539:374:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Proposition 1 links not only the mean volatility, but also dispersion in firms’ volatility, to
19:631:39764:22:21:72:540:352:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dispersion of log firm size.  Figure 2 shows a strong association between firm volatility and
19:632:39857:21:22:72:540:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:firm size dispersion, based on the market measures.  The correlation between the two time-
19:633:39947:22:22:72:540:309:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:series is 79.3%.  Appendix B.2 shows that the positive correlation between size dispersion and
19:634:40041:22:21:72:540:287:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:average volatility holds both at low and business cycle frequencies, based on HP filter trend
19:635:40134:21:22:72:540:266:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and cycle components of these time series.  Appendix B.3 reports formal Granger causality
19:636:40223:22:22:72:540:244:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tests showing that size dispersion leads mean and dispersion of the volatility distribution.
19:637:40315:22:21:72:540:222:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:These tests are useful for establishing that the time-series connection is statistically large
19:638:40409:21:22:72:263:201:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and not driven by trends in the data.
19:639:40446:22:22:89:539:179:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:It  is  important  to  note  that  the  same  relationship  between  the  moments  of  the  firm
19:640:40542:22:21:72:540:157:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:size and firm volatility distributions exists for our sales-based measures.  Figure 3 shows a
19:641:40635:21:22:72:540:136:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlation of 85.5% between mean volatility and lagged firm size dispersion.  Because the
19:642:40725:22:22:72:540:114:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sales-based data only start in the 1960s, their dynamics are more affected by the persistent
19:643:40817:22:0:72:540:92:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:increase in firm size dispersion and volatility that took place between the 1960s and the 1990s.

22:732:45633:9:47:72:523:266:8:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:F:(σS,t−1) and column four reports the correlation between dispersion in log volatility (σσ,t) and lagged log size dispersion.
22:733:45757:47:22:72:540:219:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:provided by Bloom et al. (2012), we find a strong positive correlation between stock return
22:734:45848:22:22:72:540:197:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and sales growth volatility of publicly listed firms and the volatility of log productivity and
22:735:45943:22:21:72:540:175:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of output growth for all public and private firms in manufacturing.  Sales growth volatility
22:736:46035:21:22:72:540:154:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(stock return volatility) has a 49% (33%) correlation with log TFP volatility 38% (52%) cor-
22:737:46127:22:22:72:540:132:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:relation with output growth volatility.  Third, using data from Compustat on privately held
22:738:46218:22:21:72:540:110:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:firms, we again find strong positive correlations between the time series of size dispersion,
22:739:46311:21:0:72:540:89:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:mean volatility, and volatility dispersion for public and for private firms.  The correlations
23:740:46407:0:22:72:493:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:range from 55% to 91%.  Appendix B.4 provides data sources and detailed results.
23:741:46487:22:22:89:539:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Our results focus on data for publicly listed firms because these are the firms for which
23:742:46576:22:21:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we observe customer-supplier network relationships.  The key stylized facts linking the size
23:743:46668:21:44:72:522:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and volatility distributions hold broadly and are not an artifact of public firm selection.
23:744:46759:44:29:72:297:599:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:3.4    Volatility Factor Structure
23:745:46793:29:22:72:540:570:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Recent research has documented a puzzling degree of common variation in the panel of firm-
23:746:46883:22:21:72:540:548:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:level volatilities.  Kelly, Lustig, and Nieuwerburgh (2012) show that firm-level stock return
23:747:46976:21:22:72:540:527:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatilities share a single common factor that explains roughly 35% of the variation in log
23:748:47067:22:22:72:539:505:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatilities  for  the  entire  panel  of  CRSP  stocks.   ThisR2is  nearly  twice  as  high  for  the
23:749:47169:22:21:72:540:483:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:100  Fama-French  portfolios.   They  also  show  that  this  strong  factor  structure  is  not  only
23:750:47271:21:22:72:540:462:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a feature of return volatilities, but also of sales growth volatilities.  The puzzling aspect of
23:751:47367:22:22:72:540:440:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:this result is that the factor structure remains nearly completely intact after removing all
23:752:47459:22:21:72:540:418:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:common variation in returns or sales growth rates.  Hence, common volatility dynamics are
23:753:47548:21:22:72:455:397:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:unlikely to be driven by an omitted common return or sales growth factor.
23:754:47621:22:22:89:539:375:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The  granular  network  model  predicts  a  high  degree  of  comovement  in  firm  volatility.
23:755:47716:22:21:72:540:353:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Proposition 1 shows an approximate factor structure among the volatilities of all firms, and
23:756:47808:21:22:72:540:332:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:suggests  that  concentration  of  the  lagged  economy-wide  size  distribution  is  the  appropri-
23:757:47908:22:22:72:540:310:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ate  factor.   Furthermore,  as  discussed  in  Section  2.5,  if  the  true  data  generating  process
23:758:48011:22:21:72:540:288:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is a network model,  then factor model residual volatilities will possess a similar degree of
23:759:48104:21:22:72:540:267:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:comovement as total volatilities, despite residual growth rates themselves being nearly un-
23:760:48195:22:22:72:126:245:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlated.
23:761:48206:22:21:89:539:223:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Panel A of Table 2 shows results of panel volatility regressions for three different factor
23:762:48297:21:22:72:540:202:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:models.  The left three columns use the volatility of total returns and total sales growth rates,
23:763:48394:22:22:72:540:180:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:while the right three columns use residual volatilities.  Residual volatilities are calculated in a
23:764:48493:22:21:72:540:158:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:one factor model regression of stock returns (sales growth rates) on the value-weighted market
23:765:48587:21:22:72:540:137:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:return (sales-weighted average growth rate).  In both cases, residuals have average pairwise
23:766:48679:22:22:72:540:115:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlations that are below 2% in absolute value, despite the original series having average
23:767:48771:22:21:72:540:93:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlations  over  25%  on  average.   Columns  (1)  and  (4)  consider  the  dispersion  of  lagged
23:768:48872:21:0:72:540:72:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:log market-based firm size as a factor.  Columns (2) and (5) consider the lagged weighted-
24:769:48964:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:average volatility.  This lagged cross-sectional average volatility is a natural benchmark for
24:770:49058:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:factor model comparison because it is essentially a first principal component of volatilities
24:771:49151:22:21:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and  because  the  lag  maintains  a  comparable  timing  with  the  conditioning  factor  implied
24:772:49249:21:22:72:540:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by our model.  The third column instead uses the contemporaneous average volatility as a
24:773:49337:22:22:72:540:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:factor.  Because it uses finer conditioning information, it can be considered an upper bound
24:774:49429:22:21:72:540:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on the explanatory power of a single factor.  We report panelR2values based on each factor.
24:775:49520:21:22:89:539:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Lagged size dispersion has the same degree of explanatory power as lagged average volatil-
24:776:49610:22:22:71:539:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ity.  Both factors capture about 25% of the panel variation in return volatility.  The contem-
24:777:49704:22:21:71:539:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:poraneous mean volatility explains closer to 40% of the variation.  The results for sales-based
24:778:49799:21:22:71:539:513:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility are similar: our size dispersion factor explains about 22% of the panel variation,
24:779:49892:22:22:71:539:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:close to the 23% and 24% for the lagged and contemporaneous average volatility factor.  We
24:780:49982:22:21:71:398:469:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:find the same results using volatilities of factor model residuals.
24:781:50049:21:22:89:539:448:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The model also predicts that larger firms have lower loadings on the size dispersion factor,
24:782:50141:22:22:71:539:426:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:which lowers the level of their volatilities relative to small firms, and also lowers their time-
24:783:50238:22:21:71:539:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:series volatility of volatility.  Panel B of Table 2 shows the loading of firm volatility on each
24:784:50335:21:22:71:539:383:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:factor, averaged within each quintile of the firm size distribution.  Small firms have loadings
24:785:50430:22:22:71:539:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that are 50% larger on the factor than large firms.  Panel C shows that the common factor
24:786:50519:22:21:71:539:339:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:explains  a  larger  share  of  the  volatility  dynamics  of  small  firms  than  that  of  large  firms.
24:787:50625:21:22:71:539:318:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Large  firms  have  lower  levels  of  volatility  and  also  less  variation  in  volatility.   Table  2  is
24:788:50734:22:22:71:539:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:consistent with the model’s prediction that dispersion in firm sizes predicts the entire panel
24:789:50828:22:43:71:192:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of firm-level volatilities.
24:790:50855:43:29:71:370:231:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:3.5    Determinants of Firm-level Volatility
24:791:50899:29:22:71:539:202:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:A large literature has examined the determinants of firm-level volatility on the basis of firm
24:792:50993:22:22:71:539:180:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:characteristics, including Black (1976) who proposed that differences in leverage drive het-
24:793:51085:22:21:71:539:158:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:erogeneity in firm volatility, Comin and Philippon (2006) who argue for industry competition
24:794:51177:21:22:71:539:137:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and R&D intensity, Davis et al. (2007) who emphasize age effects, and Brandt et al. (2010)
24:795:51267:22:22:71:539:115:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:who  argue  that  institutional  ownership  is  a  key  driver  of  volatility.   Our  model  predicts
24:796:51369:22:21:71:539:93:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a  negative  correlation  between  volatility  and  firm  size  and  a  positive  correlation  between
24:797:51471:21:0:71:398:72:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility and customer network concentration (out-Herfindahl).
25:798:51536:0:38:183:428:702:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f16:A:Table 2:R2of Volatility Factor Models

25:812:51979:19:12:72:540:427:10:g_d0_f17:10:g_d0_f5:F:Notes:The table reports factor model estimates for the panel of firm-year volatility observations.  In Panel
25:813:52087:12:12:72:540:415:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:A, total volatility is measured as standard deviation of daily returns within the calendar year, and residual
25:814:52196:12:12:72:540:403:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:volatility is estimated from daily regressions of firm returns on the value-weighted market portfolio within
25:815:52304:12:12:72:539:391:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the  calendar  year.   In  Panel  B,  total  volatility  in  yeartis  measured  as  standard  deviation  of  quarterly
25:816:52422:12:12:72:539:379:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:observations of year-on-year sales growth for each stock in calendar yearsttot+ 4.  Residual volatility is
25:817:52528:12:12:72:540:367:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:measured from regressions of firms sales growth on the sales-weighted average growth rate for all firms.  All
25:818:52637:12:12:72:539:355:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:volatility factor regressions take the form logσi,t=ai+bifactort+ei,t.  We consider three different volatility
25:819:52747:12:12:71:539:343:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:factors.  The first,  motivated by our network model,  is the lagged cross section standard deviation of log
25:820:52855:12:12:71:539:331:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:market equity,σS,t−1.  The second and third factors we consider are the lagged and contemporaneous cross
25:821:52959:12:47:71:490:319:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:section average log volatility,μσ,t−1andμσ,t.  We report the pooled factor modelR2in percent.
25:822:53052:47:22:89:539:272:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Table 3 reports panel regressions of firm-level log annual return volatility on size and out-
25:823:53145:22:22:71:539:250:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Herfindahl, controlling for a range of firm characteristics including log age, leverage, industry
25:824:53242:22:21:71:539:228:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:concentration, institutional holdings, and industry and cohort fixed effects.19Consistent with
25:825:53336:21:22:71:539:207:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:our model, we find that the two most important determinants of volatility are size and out-
25:826:53427:22:22:71:539:185:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Herfindahl.  Doubling the size of a firm decreases its volatility by between 12% and 16%.
25:827:53516:22:21:71:539:163:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:An  increase  of  customer  Herfindahl  from  zero  to  one  increases  volatility  by  88%  without
25:828:53616:21:22:71:539:142:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:controlling  for  size;  the  effect  is  17%  when  we  control  for  size.   Note  that  in  our  model
25:829:53721:22:22:71:539:120:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:size  and  network  concentration  convey  similar  information  since  size  determines  network
25:830:53818:22:18:71:539:98:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:structure.   Within  our  network  model,  a  supplier’s  size  and  its  customer  Herfindahl  are
25:831:53917:18:0:81:507:80:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:19Cohorts are defined by the year in which a firm first appears in the CRSP/Compustat data set.
26:832:54014:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:strongly  negatively  correlated  in  the  cross-section.   Given  that  concentration  in  the  sales
26:833:54116:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:network is measured with substantial noise, it is likely that size captures an important part
26:834:54209:22:21:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of the true network concentration effect.  Nevertheless,  the table provides strong evidence
26:835:54301:21:22:72:540:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that network concentration matters separately for firm volatility and survives the inclusion
26:836:54393:22:22:72:382:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of other well-known volatility determinants such as firm age.
26:837:54454:22:21:89:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:When we replace the out-Herfindahl (concentration of the customer network) with the in-
26:838:54541:21:22:72:540:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Herfindahl (concentration of the supplier network) in this multivariate regression, network
26:839:54632:22:22:72:540:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:concentration  is  no  longer  a  significant  determinant  of  firm-level  volatility  (untabulated).
26:840:54734:22:43:72:530:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This fact supports the upstream transmission of shocks we assume in our network model.
26:841:54820:43:30:72:306:491:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:3.6    Rise in Firm-level Volatility
26:842:54856:30:21:72:540:461:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In  an  influential  paper,  Campbell  et  al.  (2001)  highlight  the  rise  in  firm-level  volatility
26:843:54960:21:22:72:540:440:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:among publicly traded firms between the 1960s and 1990s.  Indeed, our data confirm that
26:844:55047:22:22:72:540:418:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the volatility of firm-level stock returns has increased from an average of 26% per year in
26:845:55138:22:21:72:540:396:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the 1950s to 63% per year since 1990.  This increase is also present in volatilities of residuals
26:846:55235:21:22:72:540:375:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:from a factor model for returns.  The increase in total and residual firm volatility has puzzled
26:847:55331:22:22:72:540:353:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:financial economists, who have offered both “real” and “financial” explanations.  Our stylized
26:848:55425:22:22:72:540:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:facts about the joint distribution of volatility and size, which manifest themselves similarly
26:849:55519:22:21:72:540:309:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in both fundamental and market volatility measures, seem to point in the direction of a real
26:850:55611:21:22:72:275:288:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:explanation rather than a financial one.
26:851:55651:22:22:89:539:266:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Our model predicts that average volatility should trend upward if size dispersion is doing
26:852:55741:22:21:72:540:244:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the same.  We find that accounting for changes in the size distribution nullifies the volatility
26:853:55837:21:22:72:540:223:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trends of the 1950-2000 sample highlighted by Campbell et al. (2001).  A regression of mean
26:854:55928:22:22:72:540:201:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:firm variance on our market-based measure of size dispersion (the cross-sectional dispersion
26:855:56020:22:21:72:540:179:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in  log  market  equity)  has  anR2of  51.3%.   There  is  no  anomalous  trend  in  average  firm
26:856:56118:21:0:72:507:158:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility in the post-war era after accounting for movements in the size distribution.
28:857:57898:0:34:72:535:708:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:4    Joint Dynamics of Size and Volatility Distributions
28:858:57954:34:22:72:540:674:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Section  3  documents  a  strong  statistical  association  between  the  distributions  of  size  and
28:859:58056:22:21:72:540:652:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility  that  matches  the  qualitative  predictions  of  our  model.   In  this  section  we  ask
28:860:58158:21:22:72:540:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:whether our model can provide a successful quantitative description of the joint distribu-
28:861:58248:22:22:72:540:609:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tion of data on firm size, firm volatility, and inter-firm network linkages.  In addition, the
28:862:58342:22:21:72:540:587:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:calibration allows us to address certain limitations that we face in our data, such as biases
28:863:58435:21:22:72:540:566:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:arising from public firm selection or truncation of weights below 10%.  It also allows us to
28:864:58527:22:22:72:540:544:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:add further realism to the model by building in entry and exit and introducing persistence
28:865:58617:22:21:72:540:522:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in connections (each of these features introduces challenges for an analytical description of
28:866:58710:21:22:72:540:501:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:firm volatility).  The model starts from an initial firm size distribution, but both the firm
28:867:58803:22:22:72:420:479:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:size and firm volatility distributions evolve endogenously thereafter.
28:868:58873:22:21:89:539:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We show that our simple model with purely homoscedastic shocks goes a long way towards
28:869:58959:21:22:72:540:436:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:matching the cross-sectional dispersion in firm size and volatility as well as matching the
28:870:59050:22:22:72:540:414:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:extent  of  time  variation  in  the  aggregate  moments  of  size  and  volatility.   The  limitations
28:871:59153:22:21:72:540:392:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of  the  model  are  also  informative:  the  benchmark  version  requires  more  concentration  in
28:872:59252:21:22:72:540:371:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:customer networks than we see in the data in order to fully match the volatility cross section
28:873:59346:22:22:72:540:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and the sensitivity of firm volatility to size.  Therefore, we also study a second model that
28:874:59439:22:21:72:540:327:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:allows for internal diversification within the boundaries of the firm, compared to the external
28:875:59534:21:44:72:333:306:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:diversification that we model through the network.
28:876:59584:44:29:72:188:262:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:4.1    Calibration
28:877:59602:29:30:72:429:233:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:4.1.1    Benchmark Model with Only External Diversification
28:878:59661:30:21:72:540:203:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We start by choosing the parameters listed in Table 4 for our benchmark model, which we
28:879:59748:21:39:72:147:182:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:refer to as M1.
28:880:59763:39:21:72:539:143:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f2:B:Size and Volatility ParametersThe fundamental volatility of the innovationsσεis set to
28:881:59849:21:22:72:540:122:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:0.22 and firm growth rateμgequals zero.  The former allows us to match mean firm volatility,
28:882:59941:22:22:72:540:100:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:while the latter corresponds to the full-sample growth rate in real market capitalization.  The
28:883:60036:22:0:72:540:78:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model is initialized with anN×1 firm size distributionS0, where eachSi,0is drawn from
29:884:60123:0:22:72:539:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a lognormal distribution with meanμs0= 10.20 and standard deviationσs0= 1.06.  These
29:885:60207:22:22:71:539:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:numbers  are  chosen  to  match  the  annual  cross-sectional  moments  of  the  size  distribution
29:886:60306:22:21:71:264:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:averaged over all years in our sample.
29:887:60344:21:22:89:540:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In each period, a fractionδof firms dies randomly.  Each dead firm is replaced by a new
29:888:60431:22:22:71:540:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:firm drawn from the initial firm size distribution.20These rules for the connection dynamics
29:889:60523:22:21:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and  firms’ birth  and death  completely  specify the  network  evolution and  the  growth  rate
29:890:60619:21:22:71:540:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:process in (2).  The exogenous firm destruction rateδis set to 5%, close to the time-series
29:891:60710:22:39:71:300:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:average firm exit rate in our sample of 4.2%.
29:892:60755:39:21:71:540:517:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f2:B:Network  ParametersThe  parameterγgovern  the  rate  at  which  idiosyncratic  shocks
29:893:60840:21:22:71:540:496:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:decay as they propagate through the network.  The closerγis to one, the more important
29:894:60926:22:22:71:539:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:higher-order  network  effects  are  in  determining  firms’  growth  rates.   We  setγ=  0.95  to
29:895:61024:22:21:71:355:452:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:match dispersion of firm volatility as best as possible.21
29:896:61082:21:22:89:539:431:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The linkage structure at time 0 is determined by the initial firm size distribution.  Each
29:897:61172:22:22:71:539:409:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:element of the connections matrixB0is drawn from a Bernoulli distribution withP(bi,j,0=
29:898:61259:22:21:71:539:387:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1) =pi,0as in (4).  The probability of forming a supplier-customer connection features the
29:899:61349:21:22:71:540:366:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:parameterZ, which governs the baseline likelihood of a connection for a firmi, as well as
29:900:61438:22:22:71:539:344:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the parameterζ, which governs how fast that probability decays with the number of firms
29:901:61525:22:21:71:539:322:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:B:N.  We setZ= 1.60 andζ= 0.8, which implies an expected out-degree of three for a firm of
29:902:61613:21:22:71:540:301:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:average size.  The parameterζis chosen close to one so that the expected out-degree grows
29:903:61702:22:22:71:220:279:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:withN, but at a slower rate.
29:904:61730:22:21:89:539:257:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To induce persistence in links, we modify linkage probabilities to give suppliers a relatively
29:905:61824:21:22:71:539:236:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:high probability of reconnecting to their customers from the previous period.  If firmidid
29:906:61914:22:22:71:540:214:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:not sell to customerjin periodt−1, then the probability it does so at timetis simply given
29:907:62004:22:21:71:540:192:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by equation (4).  Ifjwas a customer ofiatt−1, then the probability thatjcontinues to be
29:908:62091:21:19:71:540:171:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:C:i’s customer is min{pi,j,t+κ,1}.  Thus,κgoverns the persistence of connections.  We setκ
29:909:62179:19:16:81:539:152:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:20We have also analyzed versions of the model where there is additional endogenous exit and where new
29:910:62280:16:12:72:540:136:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:firms are drawn from the lower half of the initial size distribution.  Both features have little qualitative effect
29:911:62395:12:8:72:135:124:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:on our results.
29:912:62410:8:16:81:539:116:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:21A spatial autoregression of supplier growth rates on the weighted average growth rate of its Compustat
29:913:62514:16:11:72:540:100:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:customers results in a point estimate forγof at least 0.90 across a range of specifications.  This provides
29:914:62621:11:0:72:327:89:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:direct evidence on the plausibility of this parameter value.
