30:931:63391:39:29:72:463:376:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:4.1.2    Extended Model with Internal and External Diversification
30:932:63457:29:22:72:540:347:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We consider an extended model, M2, that allows for internal diversification effects by having
30:933:63550:22:39:72:456:325:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility of a firm’s growth rate innovation depend negatively on firm size:
30:934:63627:39:0:225:290:286:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:C:σ2ε,t=σ20−λ
30:935:63638:0:16:291:382:294:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:logSt−E[log(S)]
30:936:63653:16:0:313:361:278:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:A:E[log(S)]
30:937:63662:0:40:383:540:286:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:A:,(9)
30:938:63666:40:22:71:539:246:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:whereσ20plays  the  role  ofσ2εin  the  benchmark  model  and  the  new  coefficientλgoverns
30:939:63758:22:21:71:539:224:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the semi-elasticity of fundamental firm variance with respect to firm size.  One motivation
30:940:63849:21:22:71:539:203:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for why large firms witness less volatile shocks is internal diversification: when two stand-
30:941:63942:22:22:71:539:181:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:alone  firms  merge,  the  new  firm  is  larger  and  less  volatile  because  the  businesses  are  not
30:942:64047:22:21:71:539:159:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:perfectly  correlated  with  one  another.   We  chooseσ20=  0.40  andλ=  0.90  to  match  the
30:943:64141:21:22:72:540:138:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cross-sectional mean and standard deviation of the firm volatility distribution, holding fixed
30:944:64235:22:18:72:179:116:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:all other parameters.
30:945:64256:18:15:81:539:98:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:22In our benchmark model the average death rate of truncated links is 57% whereas the average death
30:946:64355:15:0:72:215:83:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:rate of untruncated links is 46%.
31:947:64390:0:22:89:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The extended model also changes the form of the weighting functionwi,j,t, which governs
31:948:64477:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the importance of customerjin supplieri’s network, by introducing the curvature parameter
31:949:64566:22:24:72:83:664:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:B:ψ:
31:950:64568:24:0:249:284:640:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:A:wi,j,t=
31:951:64575:0:11:305:341:650:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:C:bi,j,tSψj,t
31:952:64586:11:12:289:308:639:12:g_d0_f8:8:g_d0_f7:C:∑N
31:953:64588:12:0:301:347:627:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f6:C:k=1bi,k,tS
31:954:64598:0:9:348:354:636:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:ψ

31:956:64602:0:33:359:540:640:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f2:A:.(10)
31:957:64607:33:22:71:540:607:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:By settingψ= 0.1, we make the importance of a given customer less steep in customer size.
31:958:64696:22:22:71:540:585:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We lowerZfrom its benchmark value of 1.60 to 0.46,  increasing the expected number of
31:959:64781:22:43:71:331:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:connections for a firm of average size from 3 to 10.
31:960:64833:43:30:71:261:520:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:4.2    Simulation Procedure
31:961:64860:30:21:71:539:490:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We  simulate  our  model  for  2,000  firms  and  1,300  periods  (years).   We  discard  the  first
31:962:64960:21:22:71:539:469:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:300 observations to let the network settle down to its long run distribution, and compute
31:963:65049:22:22:71:539:447:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model statistics by averaging over the last 1,000 years.  In each period, we report moments
31:964:65140:22:21:71:539:425:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:based on the sample of the largest 1,000 firms.  We focus on the largest firms to account
31:965:65229:21:22:71:539:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for selection:  our Compustat sample is the subset of public firms, which are typically the
31:966:65320:22:22:71:539:382:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:largest firms in the economy, and these firms are connected to a large number of unobserved
31:967:65411:22:21:71:539:360:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:smaller firms.  Our choice of a total number ofN= 2,000 firms is dictated by computational
31:968:65501:21:22:71:155:339:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:considerations.23
31:969:65518:22:22:89:539:317:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The number of public firms in our data sample fluctuates over time, but our calibration
31:970:65605:22:21:71:539:295:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:considers a constant sample of 1,000 public firms.  To further improve comparability between
31:971:65697:21:22:71:539:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the model and data, we focus on the top 33% of Compustat firms by size, which in our data
31:972:65786:22:22:71:539:252:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:contains 1,000 firms on average.  For completeness, we also report the empirical moments for
31:973:65878:22:21:71:426:230:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the entire distribution of public firms (about 3,000 firms on average).
31:974:65949:21:22:89:539:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Our simulated model is well suited to deal with truncation.  We implement the truncation
31:975:66037:22:22:71:540:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:inside the model to match the truncation of the data.  That is, we treatwi,j,tas unobserved
31:976:66128:22:21:71:539:165:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:wheneverwi,j,tis below 10% in the simulation.  Since we can observe both truncated and
31:977:66214:21:22:71:539:144:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:untruncated moments of the model as calibrated to the data, we are able to make indirect
31:978:66302:22:18:71:396:122:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:inferences about the behavior of the full, untruncated economy.
31:979:66365:18:16:81:539:104:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:23The same random number generator seeds are used for each model so that,  when comparing models,
31:980:66462:16:0:72:540:88:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:differences in results are due only to differences in parameters rather than differences in random shock draws.
32:981:66575:0:22:89:539:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Finally,  in  order  to  compare  variance  moments  in  the  model  and  data,  we  take  into
32:982:66670:22:44:72:421:686:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:account the fact that empirical variances are estimated with noise.24
32:983:66739:44:29:72:416:642:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:4.3    Calibration Targets and Simulation Results
32:984:66788:29:22:72:540:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Below,  we  outline  the  features  of  the  observed  size,  volatility,  and  network  data  that  we
32:985:66891:22:38:72:474:591:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:target in our calibration and discuss how well the benchmark model fits them.
32:986:66968:38:30:72:213:553:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:4.3.1    Size Distribution
32:987:66994:30:21:72:540:523:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Table 5 reports the target moments of the cross-sectional log size distribution.  All reported
32:988:67088:21:22:72:540:502:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:moments are time-series averages over the 1926-2010 sample for size and volatility data, and
32:989:67180:22:22:72:540:480:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:over 1980-2010 for sales network data.  The first column reports moments for the full cross
32:990:67271:22:21:72:540:458:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:section of firms observed in Compustat.  The second column reports results for the top 33%
32:991:67361:21:22:72:540:437:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of Compustat firms by size in each year.  Column (2) is the main column of interest in the
32:992:67451:22:22:72:540:415:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data.  For example, 11.63 is the average log firm size of the large-firm cross section (this is
32:993:67546:22:21:72:540:393:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:naturally higher than the 9.61 mean for the full Compustat sample).  The dispersion of the
32:994:67636:21:22:72:540:372:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:log size distribution is 1.06 for the top-33% group and 1.79 for the full sample.  As can be
32:995:67728:22:22:72:540:350:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:seen from column (3), the benchmark model (M1) matches the main moments of the firm
32:996:67811:22:21:72:540:328:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:size distribution.  It matches the cross section mean and dispersion in size exactly, by virtue
32:997:67906:21:22:72:540:307:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of  the  calibration.   The  other  moments  of  the  size  distribution  constitute  over-identifying
32:998:68008:22:22:72:540:285:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:restrictions.  The inter-quartile range for log firm size is [10.79,12.25],  versus [10.78,12.25]
32:999:68105:22:21:72:540:263:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the top-33% sample.  At both extremes of the size distribution, the model also compares
32:1000:68195:21:22:72:540:242:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:favorably to the data.  Since the size distribution evolves endogenously, the network effects
32:1001:68288:22:22:72:540:220:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are crucial to generate enough heterogeneity in firm size. Firms with well-diversified customer
32:1002:68383:22:21:72:540:198:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:networks are better diversified and have lower volatility, which limits the variance of their
32:1003:68476:21:22:72:540:177:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:growth rate and hence their size next period.  On the other hand, firms with concentrated
32:1004:68565:22:22:72:463:155:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:customer networks can increase or decrease in size by a lot between periods.
32:1005:68641:22:19:89:539:133:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Panel B reports time series properties of the size distribution.  The first two rows show
32:1006:68730:19:15:81:539:114:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:24We  report  “estimated”  variance  moments  in  the  model,  which  we  compute  as  log(̂V art[gt+1])  =
32:1007:68837:15:12:72:540:99:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:log(V art[gt+1]) +et+1,  wheree∼ N(0,σ2e)  andσeis  the  time  series  average  of  the  cross-sectional  stan-
32:1008:68948:12:0:71:480:87:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:dard deviation of log(V art[gt+1]).  Our results are qualitatively insensitive to the choice ofσe.
33:1009:69048:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that size dispersion has high variability over time and is highly persistent.  Its time-series
33:1010:69142:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:standard deviation is 14% for the top-33% firms and 24% for all firms.  The third row shows
33:1011:69233:22:21:72:539:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that the cross-sectional standard deviation of sizegrowth(log size changes) is also volatile
33:1012:69325:21:22:72:540:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:over  time.   The  time  series  standard  deviation  is  15%  for  all  firms  and  11%  for  the  top-
33:1013:69429:22:22:72:540:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:33% firms.  These results confirm that the size distribution moves around considerably over
33:1014:69520:22:21:72:540:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:time.  In the benchmark model,  size dispersion also moves around substantially over time
33:1015:69609:21:22:72:540:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and is highly persistent.  The model produces the same high time-series variability in the
33:1016:69699:22:22:72:540:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:size dispersion as observed in the data (19% compared to 14% in column (2) and 24% in
33:1017:69784:22:21:72:540:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:column (1)), but understates the dispersion in growth rates (5% versus 11% in column (2)
33:1018:69872:21:22:72:540:513:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and 15% in column (1)).  The network structure is crucial for generating such a high degree
33:1019:69963:22:22:72:540:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of time series variation in the size distribution.  Shutting down the network effects in this
33:1020:70056:22:38:72:536:469:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:calibration (settingγ= 0) reduces aggregate size distribution dynamics to essentially zero.

36:1111:76304:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:network effect:  smaller firms have fewer connections, hence a more concentrated network of
36:1112:76395:22:21:72:236:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:customers, and higher volatility.
36:1113:76428:21:22:89:539:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Panel C reports time series properties of aggregate moments of the volatility distribution

36:1119:76977:21:22:72:540:513:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The  model  also  generates  substantial  variability  over  time  in  the  cross-sectional  mean  of

36:1125:77535:21:22:71:539:383:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(79% in the full sample and 76% in the large-firm sample) as well as in the model (77%).
36:1126:77623:22:22:71:539:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The last row reports the volatility of aggregate growth, computed as the volatility of the size-
36:1127:77719:22:21:71:539:339:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:weighted average of the relevant firm sample, which is 21% for all public firms and 20% for
36:1128:77810:21:22:71:539:318:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the top-33% sample.  It also is 20% in the model.  The model produces substantial time series
36:1129:77903:22:22:71:539:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility of the cross section moments, though it is somewhat understated in some cases.  All
36:1130:77997:22:21:71:539:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fluctuations in the volatility distribution rely crucially on the network structure of the model.
36:1131:78094:21:22:71:539:253:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:As with the size distribution, shutting down network effects in the simulations produces a
36:1132:78184:22:22:71:539:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility distribution whose moments are nearly constant over time.  Thus, our model with
36:1133:78274:22:22:71:539:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:only i.i.d. homoscedastic shocks endogenously generates periods with more uncertainty and
36:1134:78363:22:21:71:539:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:periods with less uncertainty.  “High uncertainty” periods are associated with a high level of
36:1135:78457:21:22:71:288:166:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:concentration in the firm size distribution.
36:1136:78501:22:22:89:539:144:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Finally, we re-estimate the volatility factor regressions of Table 2 on simulated data from
36:1137:78592:22:21:71:540:122:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the model.  The correspondingR2statistics for panel regressions of total volatility are 36%,
36:1138:78684:21:22:71:539:101:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:37%,  and  40%  for  the  three  factors  considered  in  that  table  (from  left  to  right).   These
36:1139:78787:22:0:71:539:79:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:numbers are quite close to their empirical counterparts of 24%, 26%, and 39%.  Thus, the
37:1140:78877:0:37:200:411:702:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f16:A:Table 7:Network Target Moments

37:1159:79440:24:12:72:539:393:10:g_d0_f17:10:g_d0_f5:F:Notes:All reported moments are time-series averages unless explicitly mentioned otherwise.  The first and
37:1160:79545:12:12:72:540:381:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:second  columns  report  data  for  the  cross  section  of  firms  for  which  we  have  customer  information  from
37:1161:79662:12:12:72:540:369:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Compustat.  The sample is 1980-2009.  The variance in column (1)-(2) are calculated based on daily stock
37:1162:79766:12:12:72:540:357:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:return data, while the variance in column (3)-(4) are based on 20 quarters worth of annual sales growth (in
37:1163:79873:12:12:72:540:345:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the current and the next four years).  Column (5) reports the corresponding moments for the benchmark
37:1164:79974:12:12:72:540:333:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:model M1.  Column (6) reports the moments for the extended model M2.  Log variances in the model are
37:1165:80074:12:12:72:540:321:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:constructed with added estimation noise to make them comparable to the moments in the data.  All model
37:1166:80176:12:47:72:398:309:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:calculations impose the 10% truncation on network weightswi,juniformly.
37:1167:80247:47:39:72:415:262:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:model quantitatively replicates the factor structure in volatilities.25
37:1168:80318:39:29:72:222:223:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:4.3.3    Network Moments
37:1169:80342:29:22:72:540:194:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Table  7  reports  calibration  results  for  network  connectivity  and  concentration.   Panel  A
37:1170:80441:22:18:72:415:172:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:focuses on out-degrees and the bottom panel focuses on in-degrees.
37:1171:80507:18:15:81:539:154:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:25In untabulated analyses we run Granger Causality tests of volatility moments on lagged size dispersion
37:1172:80611:15:12:72:540:139:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:in the simulated model and find very similar results to Granger tests on actual data reported in Appendix
37:1173:80716:12:0:72:90:127:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:B.3.
38:1174:80722:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f2:B:Out-DegreesColumn (5) of Table 7 shows the network-related properties of the bench-
38:1175:80805:22:22:71:539:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:mark model.  Like the data in columns (1) and (2), the model features a small number of
38:1176:80892:22:21:71:539:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:supplier-customer relationships after truncation:  the median is 1.71 in the model and 1.00
38:1177:80983:21:22:71:539:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the data.  The 99thpercentile of the truncated out-degree distribution is 4.66 in the model
38:1178:81077:22:22:71:539:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:compared to 3.32 in the data.  Truncation severely affects the out-degrees: the median of the
38:1179:81170:22:21:71:531:599:12:g_d0_f15:8:g_d0_f3:C:untruncatedout-degree distribution is 3.71 in the model while the 99thpercentile is 112.26
38:1180:81260:21:22:89:539:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Next  we  turn  to  customer  network  concentration  or  out-Herfindahls.   The  Herfindahl
38:1181:81352:22:22:72:540:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:indices, which are also based on truncated degree information, are less biased because large
38:1182:81444:22:21:72:540:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:customers  receive  a  large  weight  and  are  more  likely  to  be  in  the  database.   The  main
38:1183:81544:21:22:72:540:513:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shortcoming  of  the  benchmark  calibration  is  that  customer  network  concentration  (out-
38:1184:81639:22:22:72:540:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Herfindahl) is too high.  The median is 0.60, higher than the 0.05 value we observe in the
38:1185:81729:22:21:72:540:469:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data.  The reasons are that in M1 suppliers typically have a small number of connections
38:1186:81817:21:22:72:540:448:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and  the  weighting  function  is  linear  in  customer  size,  making  large  customers  extremely
38:1187:81916:22:22:72:540:426:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:important  in  a  suppliers  network.   To  generate  substantial  dispersion  in  firm  volatilities
38:1188:82017:22:21:72:540:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in a setting with only uncorrelated shocks,  the model requires too much concentration in
38:1189:82106:21:22:72:529:383:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:customer networks.  The extended model M2 discussed below addresses this shortcoming.
38:1190:82191:22:22:89:539:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The fifth row of Panel A reports the correlation between out-degree and supplier size.  As
38:1191:82281:22:21:72:540:339:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:discussed in Section 2.6, we find a nearly zero correlation between truncated out-degree and
38:1192:82373:21:22:72:540:318:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:size in the data.  Because of truncation in the network data, the zero correlation based on
38:1193:82464:22:22:72:540:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:truncated degrees belies a substantial positive correlation between size and true, untruncated
38:1194:82558:22:21:72:540:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:out-degrees.  Our simulation results show that large firms have many more connections than
38:1195:82648:21:22:72:540:253:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:small firms, with a cross-sectional correlation of 34% between the truncated out-degree and
38:1196:82739:22:22:72:539:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:log size in the model, and a 60% correlation betweenuntruncatedout-degree and log size.
38:1197:82826:22:22:72:540:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This  implies  that  truncation  downward  biases  the  correlation  estimate  by  26  percentage
38:1198:82923:22:21:72:540:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:points.  Using the model-implied bias to correct the observed correlations in the data (1% for
38:1199:83017:21:22:72:540:166:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the full sample and−7% for the top-33% sample) implies a 19% to 27% correlation between
38:1200:83104:22:22:72:319:144:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:untruncated out-degree and log size in the data.
38:1201:83152:22:15:89:539:122:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Two  moments  that  lie  at  the  heart  of  the  model’s  mechanism  are  the  cross-sectional
38:1202:83247:15:16:81:539:107:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:26To the extent the data permit, Appendix B.1 discusses the effect of truncation on the distributions of
38:1203:83351:16:0:72:300:91:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the out-degrees and the out-Herfindahls in the data.
39:1204:83405:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlations of the out-Herfindahl with size and of the out-Herfindahl with variance.  The
39:1205:83495:22:22:72:539:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model generates a strong negative correlation between out-degree Herfindahl and size (−71%)
39:1206:83586:22:21:71:539:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and a positive correlation between out-degree Herfindahl and volatility (54%), arising because
39:1207:83680:21:22:71:539:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:large firms have better diversified and thus less concentrated customer bases.  In the data, we
39:1208:83775:22:22:71:539:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:find a−31% correlation between size and out-Herfindahl.  We also find a positive relationship
39:1209:83868:22:21:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:between supplier log variance and out-Herfindahl:  the correlation is 15% based on returns
39:1210:83958:21:22:71:539:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and  29%  based  on  sales.   That  is,  firms  with  a  more  diversified  customer  network  indeed
39:1211:84059:22:22:71:539:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:experience lower volatility because they are better insulated against a shock to any single
39:1212:84150:22:21:71:539:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:customer.  Appendix B.1 shows that these three correlations are statistically significant at
39:1213:84242:21:22:71:540:513:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the 1% level.  Similar to the firm-level evidence, it also finds a−39% correlation between an
39:1214:84335:22:22:71:539:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:industry’s log size and its out-Herfindahl and a 22% correlation between its out-Herfindahl
39:1215:84426:22:38:71:160:469:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and log volatility.
39:1216:84445:38:22:71:539:431:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f2:B:In-DegreesTurning to Panel B of Table 7, the median truncated number of suppliers, or
39:1217:84530:22:22:71:540:409:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in-degree, is 1 while the 99thpercentile is 17 or 32 depending on the data sample.  In the
39:1218:84620:22:21:71:540:387:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:benchmark model, the median in-degree is 1.90 and the 99thpercentile is between 6 and 8.
39:1219:84708:21:22:72:540:366:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Concentration  of  supplier  networks  is  lower  than  that  of  customer  networks,  but  still  too
39:1220:84810:22:22:72:200:344:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:high relative to the data.
39:1221:84836:22:21:89:539:322:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f6:A:In the model, the probability that a connection exists between supplieriand customerj
39:1222:84921:21:22:72:539:301:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:depends only oni’s size and is independent of firmj’s size.  Thus, the model predicts a zero
39:1223:85013:22:22:72:539:279:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlation between size and in-degree in theabsenceof link truncation.  While we assume
39:1224:85101:22:21:72:540:257:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:smaller  firms  are  equally  likely  to  be  customers  as  large  firms,  truncation  will  eliminate
39:1225:85204:21:22:72:540:236:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the  links  with  small  customers,  leaving  large  firms  counted  as  customers  far  more  often.
39:1226:85305:22:22:72:540:214:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Thus  we  expect  to  see  a  strong  association  between  size  and  in-degree  in  the  truncated
39:1227:85405:22:21:72:540:192:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data  (we  find  a  correlation  of  37%).   Indeed,  the  benchmark  model  M1  produces  a  49%
39:1228:85502:21:22:72:540:171:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlation between size and in-degree when the simulated samples are truncated.  However,
39:1229:85592:22:22:72:539:149:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the correlation betweenuntruncatedin-degree and size is 4%, which implies that the 37%
39:1230:85678:22:21:72:540:127:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlation estimate in the data is severely upward biased by truncation.  The magnitude
39:1231:85766:21:22:72:539:106:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of  the  bias  is  such  that  the  bias-corrected  correlation  betweenuntruncatedin-degree  and
39:1232:85863:22:0:72:540:84:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:log size in the data is close to zero for both data samples, and provides evidence that the
40:1233:85956:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:likelihood of a connection between a supplier and a customer does not depend strongly on
40:1234:86044:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the customer’s size, as assumed in our model.  Finally, we find a modestly positive correlation
40:1235:86139:22:21:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:between in-Herfindahl and both measures of volatility, though these estimates are statistically
40:1236:86234:21:39:72:383:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:insignificant in the data (t-statistics shown in the appendix).
40:1237:86297:39:29:72:463:604:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:4.3.4    Extended Model with Internal and External Diversification
40:1238:86363:29:22:72:540:575:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The benchmark model calibration with only external (network) diversification implies too
40:1239:86451:22:22:72:540:553:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:much concentration and fails to generate enough dispersion in firm volatility.  The extended
40:1240:86543:22:21:72:539:531:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model  (M2)  with  internal  diversification  jointly  matches  size,  volatility,andnetwork  mo-
40:1241:86640:21:22:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ments.  This model can generate a wider firm volatility spread than the benchmark model
40:1242:86727:22:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:while continuing to generate a firm size distribution that is similar to the data.  In partic-
40:1243:86821:22:21:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ular, M2 generates time-series correlations between firm size dispersion and the mean and
40:1244:86910:21:22:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dispersion of firm variance that are qualitatively similar to the data.  Average firm volatility
40:1245:87006:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:has a 52% standard deviation, compared to 20% in M1 and 64% in the data.  In general, M2
40:1246:87094:22:21:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:produces much more action in aggregate moments of the size and volatility distributions.
40:1247:87182:21:22:72:540:380:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Firm size and volatility are cross-sectionally more negatively correlated with each other in
40:1248:87274:22:22:72:540:358:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:M2 than in M1, bringing the model closer to the data, noting that part of this correlation is
40:1249:87367:22:21:72:207:336:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:now mechanically built in.
40:1250:87393:21:22:89:539:315:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Most importantly, the truncated network moments from M2 are much closer to the data
40:1251:87476:22:22:72:540:293:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:than  those  of  M1.   The  median  out-Herfindahl  is  0.12,  a  factor  of  5  smaller  than  in  M1,
40:1252:87579:22:21:72:540:271:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and  much  closer  to  the  data.   In  addition,  the  truncated  number  of  suppliers  (in-degree)
40:1253:87680:21:22:72:540:250:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and the supplier network concentration (in-Herfindahl) are a closer fit with the data than
40:1254:87770:22:22:72:540:228:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the benchmark model.27Thus, the model matches the key features of the size and volatility
40:1255:87859:22:21:72:540:206:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:distributions while respecting the observed degree of concentration in customer and supplier
40:1256:87951:21:22:72:121:185:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:networks.
40:1257:87960:22:22:89:539:163:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The internal diversification effect, modeled as an exogenous negative correlation between
40:1258:88049:22:17:72:540:141:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shock volatility and firm size, cannot match the data in the absence of network effects.  In
40:1259:88141:17:16:81:539:124:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:27While  the  correlation  between  log  size  and  truncated  out-degree  (number  of  customers)  is−45%  in
40:1260:88251:16:12:72:540:108:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:M2 in row 5 of Panel A, the correlation between log size and untruncated out-degree is +74%.  Thus, the
40:1261:88354:12:12:72:540:96:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:truncation bias for this correlation is much larger in M2 than in M1.  The bias in the correlation between
40:1262:88460:12:12:72:540:84:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:log size and in-degree (number of suppliers) is smaller in M2 than in M1; the correlation between log size
40:1263:88566:12:0:72:432:72:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:and untruncated in-degrees is similar in both models (0.06 in M2 and 0.04 in M1).
41:1264:88649:0:22:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:particular, the correlation between firm size dispersion and mean firm volatility turns from
41:1265:88741:22:22:72:540:686:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:positive to negative once we shut down the network effects in M2 (settingγ= 0).  Without
41:1266:88829:22:21:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:network transmission, an increase in the firm size dispersion makes the firms in the top half
41:1267:88922:21:22:72:540:643:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of the distribution (public firms) larger, which lowers their average volatility by assumption.
41:1268:89017:22:22:72:539:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Hence, the network effects aresolelyresponsible for the strong positive correlation between
41:1269:89108:22:21:72:540:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:size dispersion and mean volatility,  our main moment of interest.  This result underscores
41:1270:89199:21:22:72:540:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the crucial role of network effects in accounting for the firm volatility facts, over and above
41:1271:89294:22:39:72:225:556:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:internal diversification effects.
41:1272:89327:39:29:72:268:517:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:4.3.5    Downstream Transmission
41:1273:89359:29:22:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Our paper also raises an interesting question on the direction of shock propagation in eco-
41:1274:89450:22:21:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:nomic networks.  The literature studies how inter-sector trade networks influence aggregate
41:1275:89541:21:22:72:539:445:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:volatility and typically considersdownstreamtransmission of shocks from intermediate goods
41:1276:89631:22:22:72:540:423:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:producers to final goods producers.  Our results suggest that upstream shock propagation
41:1277:89719:22:21:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:provides  a  better  description  of  firm  volatility  data,  as  in  equilibrium  models  with  final
41:1278:89822:21:22:72:226:380:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:demand shocks (Shea (2002)).
41:1279:89850:22:22:89:539:358:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To  better  understand  the  difference  in  predictions,  Appendix  C  studies  a  version  of
41:1280:89945:22:21:72:540:336:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:our model with downstream transmission of shocks instead of upstream transmission.  All
41:1281:90032:21:22:72:540:315:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:parameters  are  the  same  as  those  in  M1.   Interestingly,  the  downstream  model  delivers
41:1282:90129:22:22:72:540:293:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:identical size and volatility moments, highlighting the usefulness of network mechanisms for
41:1283:90221:22:21:72:526:271:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:describing joint size and volatility facts, regardless of the direction of shock propagation.
41:1284:90314:21:22:89:539:250:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The network moments, however, help identify the transmission direction.  The in-degree
41:1285:90400:22:22:72:539:228:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and  out-degree  moments  are  reversed  between  upstream  and  downstream  models.28The
41:1286:90489:22:21:72:540:206:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:downstream model matches the correlations between in-Herfindahl and size and volatility
41:1287:90576:21:22:72:540:185:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:better, but completely misses the correlation between out-Herfindahl on the one hand and
41:1288:90664:22:22:72:540:163:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:size  and  volatility  on  the  other  hand.   In  the  data,  the  correlation  between  volatility  and
41:1289:90769:22:21:72:540:141:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:out-Herfindahl is statistically much stronger than that with in-Herfindahl, as in the model
41:1290:90860:21:18:72:250:120:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with upstream shock transmission.
41:1291:90893:18:16:81:539:102:7:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f5:F:28The statement is true for untruncated in-degree and out-degree moments.  Truncation affects the network
41:1292:90998:16:0:72:284:86:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:moments; see appendix for a detailed discussion.
42:1293:91048:0:34:72:194:708:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:5    Conclusion
42:1294:91063:34:22:72:540:674:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We document new features of the joint evolution of the firm size and firm volatility distri-
42:1295:91155:22:21:72:540:652:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:bution  and  propose  a  new  model  to  account  for  these  features.   In  the  model,  shocks  are
42:1296:91257:21:22:72:540:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:transmitted upstream from customers to suppliers.  Firms sell products to an imperfectly
42:1297:91345:22:22:72:540:609:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:diversified portfolio of customers.  The larger the supplier, the more customer connections
42:1298:91436:22:21:72:540:587:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the supplier has, the better diversified it is and the lower its volatility.  Large customers have
42:1299:91534:21:22:72:540:566:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a relatively strong influence on their suppliers, so shocks to large firms have an important
42:1300:91626:22:22:72:231:544:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:effect throughout the economy.
42:1301:91656:22:21:89:539:522:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:When the size dispersion increases in this economy, large firms become more important
42:1302:91741:21:22:72:540:501:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and many customer networks become less diversified.  In those times, average firm volatility
42:1303:91833:22:22:72:540:479:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is higher as is the cross-sectional dispersion of volatility.  Because the underlying innovations
42:1304:91930:22:21:72:540:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are  i.i.d.   over  time,  the  model  endogenously  generates  “uncertainty  shocks.”  We  provide
42:1305:92029:21:22:72:540:436:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:direct evidence of network linkages and use supplier-customer relationship data to discipline
42:1306:92122:22:22:72:540:414:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the calibration of our model.  A calibrated version of the network model augmented with
42:1307:92209:22:21:72:540:392:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:internal diversification effects quantitatively replicates the most salient features of the firm
42:1308:92305:21:22:72:540:371:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:size and the volatility distributions;  without network effects,  the model cannot match the
42:1309:92397:22:0:72:98:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data.

49:1809:101934:32:18:72:355:250:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:B.1    Summary Statistics Network Data
49:1810:101972:18:12:72:343:232:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:This appendix provides additional detail on the network data.
49:1811:102033:12:12:89:539:220:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:We find that the number of customers a Compustat firm has, the “out-degrees,” ranges between 1 and
49:1812:102131:12:12:72:540:208:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:24, while the number of suppliers a firm has, the “in-degrees,” ranges between 1 and 130.  Firms can but are
49:1813:102239:12:12:72:540:196:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:not required to report customers that represent less than 10% of their sales.  Out-degrees can reach 24 since
49:1814:102348:12:12:72:540:184:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:some suppliers (23%) voluntarily report customers that fall below the 10% sales threshold.  The maximum
49:1815:102451:12:12:72:540:172:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:out-degrees falls to 5 when we strictly impose the 10% sales truncation.  Figure 4 provides a summary of
49:1816:102555:12:12:72:540:160:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:network connections for customers and suppliers in the Compustat linkage data.  The left panel shows the
49:1817:102659:12:12:72:540:148:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:distribution of number of links by supplier (out-degree) on a log-log scale, while the right panel shows the
49:1818:102767:12:12:72:268:136:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:distribution of links by customer (in-degree).
49:1819:102813:12:12:89:539:124:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Figure 5 reports histograms of weights of customer-supplier sales linkages pooling all supplier-year obser-
49:1820:102920:12:12:72:540:112:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:vations.  The distribution for the raw data, in which some suppliers voluntarily report customers below the
49:1821:103027:12:12:72:540:100:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:10% sales threshold, is on the left.  The right panel shows the weight distribution when we strictly impose
49:1822:103134:12:0:72:182:88:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the 10% sales truncation.

50:1845:103596:46:12:89:539:422:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Table 8 reports some key moments of the network data.  We calculate cross-sectional correlations for
50:1846:103696:12:12:72:540:410:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:each yearly network realization in the Compustat linkage data and report the time series average of annual
50:1847:103802:12:12:72:540:398:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:correlations.  We also report a t-statistic, measured as the ratio of the time series mean of the correlation to
50:1848:103914:12:12:72:540:386:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:its time-series standard deviation.  In these calculations, size is defined as annual firm sales.  Columns (1)-(5)
50:1849:104028:12:12:72:540:374:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:take the perspective of the suppliers and the connections with their customers.  Instead, Columns (6)-(10)
50:1850:104134:12:12:72:540:362:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:focus on costumers’ connections to their suppliers.  In Panel A we consider all customer-supplier linkages,
50:1851:104241:12:12:72:540:350:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:while in Panel B we only keep those pairs where the customer represents at least 10% of the supplier’s sales.
50:1852:104350:12:12:72:540:338:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Thus, Panel B imposes the truncation also on the firms that voluntarily report more customer data than
50:1853:104452:12:12:72:111:326:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:required.
50:1854:104461:12:12:89:539:314:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Panel C adds moments obtained from industry data.  Industry input-output data are from the Bureau
50:1855:104558:12:12:72:540:302:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:of Economic Analysis (BEA). Because industry definitions vary quite dramatically over time, we focus on a
50:1856:104663:12:12:72:540:290:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:set of 65 industries we can track consistently over time between 1998 and 2011.  These data are informative
50:1857:104770:12:12:72:540:278:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:for  evaluating  cross-sectional  correlations  between  industry  size  and  network  structure  since  they  do  not
50:1858:104888:12:12:72:540:266:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:suffer the truncation issue that plagues the Compustat firm-level data.  In related work, Ahern and Harford
50:1859:104995:12:12:72:540:254:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:(forthcoming) use the network topography implied by the BEA industry data to show that the properties
50:1860:105096:12:12:72:540:242:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:of these networks have a bearing on the incidence of cross-industry mergers.  We do not have return-based
50:1861:105201:12:11:72:247:230:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:volatility measures in the industry data.
50:1862:105242:11:12:89:539:219:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:The moments reported in columns 1 and 2 are discussed in Section 2.6, and the moments reported in
50:1863:105339:12:12:72:540:207:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the other columns are discussed in Section 4.3.3.  The numbers reported in the latter section in Table 7 are
50:1864:105447:12:32:72:469:195:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:identical to those reported in Panel B of Table 8.  Table 8 additionally provides t-statistics.

51:1895:107867:46:12:72:540:348:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:and size dispersion are 65.9% for the market-based measure and 54.4% for the sales-based measure.  These
51:1896:107971:12:12:72:540:336:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:results suggest that the correlations between the dispersion in the firm size distribution and moments of the
51:1897:108080:12:32:72:374:324:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:volatility distribution occur at both lower and at cyclical frequencies.
51:1898:108152:32:18:72:283:292:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:B.3    Granger Causality Tests
51:1899:108182:18:12:72:540:274:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Our network model predicts that movements in the size distribution precede changes in the volatility distri-
51:1900:108290:12:12:72:540:262:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:bution. When the size distribution spreads out (contracts) at timet, the network structure for the subsequent
51:1901:108399:12:12:72:540:250:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:period adjusts, and diversification of growth rate shocks is hindered (enhanced).  We use Granger causality
51:1902:108506:12:12:72:540:238:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:tests to formally evaluate whether dispersion in firm sizes predicts the mean and standard deviation of the
51:1903:108613:12:12:72:540:226:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:volatility distribution, after controlling for own lags of the dependent variable.  Table 9 presents the results
51:1904:108725:12:12:72:540:214:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:from these tests.  We find that firm size dispersion (based on log market equity) has statistically significant
51:1905:108836:12:12:72:540:202:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:predictive power for mean firm volatility (based on returns), and dispersion in volatility.  The reverse is not
51:1906:108947:12:12:72:540:190:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:true.  After controlling for own lags of size moments, moments of the volatility distribution do not predict the
51:1907:109059:12:12:72:540:178:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:size distribution.  Lagged dispersion in log volatility appears to Granger-cause size dispersion, but the coef-
51:1908:109170:12:12:72:540:166:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f17:F:ficient has the wrong sign.  The hypothesis has the one-sided alternative that volatility dispersionpositively
51:1909:109280:12:12:72:540:154:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:predicts size dispersion; thus this negative result leads us to fail to reject the null.  This evidence suggests
51:1910:109392:12:12:72:540:142:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:that size dispersion leads the volatility distribution, consistent with the model.  This evidence should not be
51:1911:109503:12:0:72:450:130:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:interpreted as support for economic causality, but merely for time-series predictability.

52:1956:110187:46:19:72:518:328:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:B.4    Size and Volatility Moments for Public vs.  Private Firms
52:1957:110251:19:11:72:540:309:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:We investigate whether the properties of firm size and firm volatility are the same for publicly listed and
52:1958:110358:11:12:72:540:298:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:privately held firms using three different databases.  To the extent that they are, it suggests that our results
52:1959:110470:12:12:72:296:286:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:are not driven by our study of publicly-listed firms.
52:1960:110523:12:12:89:539:274:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:First, we use the data set provided by Bloom et al. (2012), which allows us to compare measures of firm-
52:1961:110627:12:12:72:540:262:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:level volatility between the universe of publicly listed firms and the universe of manufacturing establishments.
52:1962:110739:12:12:72:540:250:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Data on manufacturing establishments are from the Census of Manufactures (CM) and the Annual Survey of
52:1963:110841:12:12:72:540:238:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Manufactures (ASM) from the U.S. Census Bureau between 1972 and 2009 for establishments with 25 years
52:1964:110942:12:12:72:540:226:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:or more of observations.  The data also contain a volatility measure for all public firms with 25 years (300
52:1965:111050:12:12:72:540:214:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:months) or more in CRSP between 1960 and 2010.  Panel A of Table 10 shows the correlation of sales growth
52:1966:111155:12:12:72:540:202:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:volatility and stock return volatility with the volatility of TFP shocks and the volatility of output growth,
52:1967:111264:12:12:72:540:190:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:respectively.  IQR of log(TFP) shock (all manufact.)  is the cross-sectional interquartile range of the Total
52:1968:111373:12:12:72:540:178:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Factor  Productivity  (TFP)  “shock”  measured  at  the  establishment  level,  and  IQR  of  output  growth  (all
52:1969:111487:12:12:72:540:166:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:manufact.)  is the interquartile range of plants’ sales growth.  These two volatility measures are constructed
52:1970:111597:12:12:72:540:154:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:from the Census data.  IQR of sales growth (public) is the annual average of the interquartile range of firms’
52:1971:111707:12:12:72:540:142:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:sales growth by quarter for all publicly traded firms in Compustat between 1962 and 2010.  Lastly, IQR of
52:1972:111812:12:12:72:540:130:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:stock returns (public) is the annual average interquartile range of firms’ monthly stock returns for all public
52:1973:111923:12:12:72:540:118:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:firms in CRSP between 1960 and 2010.  Panel A shows a strong positive correlation between the sales-based
52:1974:112028:12:12:72:539:106:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:and return-based volatility measures forpublicfirms, which our study shares with Bloom et al. (2012), and
52:1975:112133:12:12:71:540:94:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f17:F:the volatility measures for output growth and TFP shocks, which are constructed from the universe ofprivate

53:1991:112817:10:46:72:401:411:8:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:F:volatility measure constructed from stock returns and the market equity measure of size.
53:1992:112905:46:12:89:539:365:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Our second data set compares size and volatility moments of Income Statement and Balance Sheet items
53:1993:113005:12:12:72:540:353:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:for the universe of active and inactive firms on Compustat.  Inactive Compustat firms are firms that are no
53:1994:113112:12:12:72:540:341:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:longer publicly-listed due to acquisition or merger, bankruptcy, leveraged buyout or change of status to a
53:1995:113218:12:12:72:540:329:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:private company.  We use quarterly data from 1962 to 2007, two different measures of size (net sales and
53:1996:113322:12:12:72:540:317:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:pretax income), and we adjust semi-annual and annual reports to quarterly data.  There are 25,619 unique
53:1997:113426:12:12:72:540:305:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:firms in the sample, and the number of active and inactive firm-quarter observations is similar.  For example,
53:1998:113536:12:12:72:540:293:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:for  net  sales  we  have  on  average  2,265  active  firms  each  period  and  3,530  inactive  firms.   Size  dispersion
53:1999:113659:12:12:72:540:281:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:is  computed  as  the  cross  section  standard  deviation  of  size.   To  calculate  the  volatility,  we  construct  the
53:2000:113782:12:12:72:540:269:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:annual growth rate, quarter by quarter, and, then, we define the volatility as the standard deviation of the
53:2001:113890:12:12:72:540:257:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:growth rate over the next 20 quarters.  Panel B of Table 10 reports the time series correlation between active
53:2002:114000:12:11:72:540:245:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:and inactive firms’ size dispersion, mean volatility and volatility dispersion.  The public and private samples
53:2003:114111:11:12:72:484:234:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:display a strong positive correlation in our key moments of the size and volatility distribution.
53:2004:114208:12:12:89:539:222:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Third, we look at the evolution of firm size dispersion for public firms versus that for all firms, using the
53:2005:114317:12:12:72:540:210:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:number of employees as the measure firm size.  The public data are from Compustat while the firm data for
53:2006:114422:12:12:72:540:198:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the universe of firms are from the Census’ Business Dynamic Statistics.  The sample is annual and covers the
53:2007:114530:12:12:72:539:186:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:period 1977-2009.  The Census reports employment data in 12 employment bins ranging from 1−4 employees
53:2008:114632:12:12:72:539:174:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:at the low end to 10,000+ at the high end.  We construct the same bins using the Compustat employment
53:2009:114733:12:12:71:539:162:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:data.  We also create a spliced series that divides the 10,000+ bin into 25 sub-bins applying an imputation
53:2010:114840:12:12:71:539:150:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:that replicates the employment distribution in that bin in the Compustat employment data.  The evolution
53:2011:114944:12:12:71:539:138:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:of firm size dispersion when considering the entire universe of firms seems similar to the one in Compustat.
53:2012:115052:12:12:71:539:126:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:The correlation between the cross-sectional variance of log size in Compustat and the spliced series is 62%.
53:2013:115160:12:12:71:480:114:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:The correlation between the non-spliced Census measure and the Compustat measure is 65%.
53:2014:115248:12:12:89:539:102:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:As  an  aside,  we  note  that  at  the  start  of  the  sample,  the  Census  reports  728  firms  with  more  than
53:2015:115364:12:12:71:539:90:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:10,000 employees, while Compustat reports 677.  So, we have fairly comprehensive coverage at the start of
53:2016:115469:12:0:71:539:78:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:the  sample.   At  the  end  of  the  sample,  there  are  1,975  with  10,000+  firms,  only  1,016  of  which  show  up

54:2037:116921:12:12:89:539:356:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:In conclusion, the changes in the firm size and volatility distribution that we have documented do not
54:2038:117023:12:12:72:540:344:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:seem specific to the universe of publicly traded firms.  Combined with the evidence for the evidence of the

54:2041:117352:12:37:72:94:309:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:data.
54:2042:117357:37:22:72:479:272:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:C    Calibration Appendix:  Downstream Results
54:2043:117403:22:12:72:540:250:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:We study a version of our model where the direction of shock transmission is reversed from upstream to
54:2044:117505:12:12:72:540:238:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:downstream.  A firm’s growth rate now depends on its own shock and on the growth rate of its suppliers.  We
54:2045:117612:12:12:72:540:226:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:use the exact same calibration as in the benchmark model.  If we abstract from truncation, then reversing the
54:2046:117721:12:12:72:540:214:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:direction only changes the network moments reported in Table 7; the moments in the size and volatility tables
54:2047:117830:12:12:72:540:202:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:5 and 6 are identical.  In terms of network moments,  the untruncated in-degree and out-degree moments
54:2048:117932:12:12:72:540:190:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:are reversed.  However, after truncation (which operates at the level of suppliers), the results look different.
54:2049:118044:12:12:72:540:178:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Table 11 reports results for the downstream transmission case.  This version of the model has more success
54:2050:118150:12:12:72:540:166:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:matching  the  in-degree  moments,  but  then  it  does  considerably  worse  matching  the  out-degree  moments
54:2051:118262:12:12:72:540:154:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:in  the  top  panel.   Most  importantly,  this  version  of  the  model  implies  counter-factually  that  there  is  no
54:2052:118383:12:12:72:540:142:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:correlation between out-Herfindahls and volatility.  In the data, we found that this relation is statistically
54:2053:118493:12:12:72:540:130:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:strong (see Table 8) unlike that between volatility and in-Herfindahl.  In addition, Table 3 showed that out-
54:2054:118602:12:12:72:540:118:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:Herfindahl is a robust determinant of firm volatility after controlling for other variables, while the relation
54:2055:118713:12:0:72:290:106:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f5:F:between supplier Herfindahls and volatility is not.
